Hadd and Rasm
intermediate
5 min
Conditions of a Valid Definition: Coextensiveness and Counterextensiveness
Ibn Taymiyya lays out the conditions for a valid definition: ittiraad and in'ikaas.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the conditions of coextensiveness (ittiraad) and counterextensiveness (in'ikaas) to assess whether a proposed definition is valid according to Ibn Taymiyya.

Core Idea
A valid definition must cover all members and only those members.

Ibn Taymiyya does not reject definition entirely; he provides a method to construct valid definitions. The key is the necessary description (wasf lazim) that satisfies two conditions: coextensiveness (ittiraad) and counterextensiveness (in'ikaas). This lesson shows how to use these conditions to test definitions in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Understand the problem
Ibn Taymiyya asks: what law (qanun) do we use to capture a definition that properly distinguishes the defined? He answers: through a necessary description (wasf lazim).
The term 'wasf lazim' (necessary description) is crucial.
2
Define coextensiveness (ittiraad)
Coextensiveness means the definition is present whenever the defined is present. The defined 'accompanies' the definition (al-mahdud ma‘a al-hadd).
3
Define counterextensiveness (in'ikaas)
Counterextensiveness means the definition is absent whenever the defined is absent. The absence of the definition entails absence of the defined.
He also says 'الإنعكاس هو انتفاء المحدود مع الحد' (counterextensiveness is the negation of the defined with the definition).
4
Apply the analogy of land boundaries
Just as the boundary of a plot of land (حد الأرض) is defined by directions (e.g., southern boundary, eastern boundary), and this includes all of the land and excludes neighboring land, so too the definition of a species must include all instances and exclude everything else.
This shows that definition is a matter of inclusion and exclusion.
5
Formulate the test for a valid definition
Given a proposed definition with a necessary description, check if it applies to all instances of the defined (ittiraad) and does not apply to anything else (in'ikaas). If both hold, the definition is valid.

Commentary
This passage presents Ibn Taymiyya's critique of the Aristotelian theory of definition (hadd). The author first summarizes Ibn Taymiyya's argument that definition does not produce new conceptual knowledge (tasawwur) of the defined. Just as a mere name (ism) does not generate conception of the named for one who has no prior acquaintance, so too definition, being a type of name, cannot generate a new conception. Consequently, the purpose of definition is not to convey the essence (kunn or mahiyya) as the Aristotelians claimed, but only to distinguish the defined from other things. Ibn Taymiyya concedes that definition may alert the mind to a concept already possessed, but that is the same function as a name. Having established that definition serves only to distinguish, Ibn Taymiyya asks for the method (qanun) to capture a proper definition. He answers that it occurs through a necessary description (wasf lazim) that is both coextensive (tard) and counterextensive (‘aks), meaning that the description is present whenever the defined is present and absent whenever the defined is absent. This is the method of the Muslim theoreticians (nuzzar) of all schools. Ibn Taymiyya cites al-Juwayni's agreement that coextensiveness and counterextensiveness are conditions for a correct definition. Ibn Taymiyya clarifies further: coextensiveness (ittiraad) means that the defined is always accompanied by the definition; counterextensiveness (in‘ikaas) means that absence of the definition entails absence of the defined. He then draws an analogy: just as the physical boundary of a plot of land distinguishes it by directions, so the definition of a species distinguishes it by attributes. The definition, like the name, serves to include all instances of the defined and exclude everything else. The author then explains that this approach grounds definition in a linguistic (lughawi) framework. The benefit of definition is to clarify the meaning of a name (misma al-ism). Since naming is a conventional linguistic act, definition becomes a matter of translation: either translating a name for someone who knows the thing but not the word, or pointing to the thing itself or its likeness for someone unfamiliar with the thing. Hence definition is of two types: definition of the name (hadd al-ism) and definition of the named (hadd al-musamma). Conception of the defined (tasawwur al-mahdud) is not the role of definition; it requires a separate demonstration (dalil) to prove the correctness of the speaker's speech. The passage concludes by stating the methodological role of definition: it is translation from one expression to another, applicable in fields like exegesis (tafsir) of the Qur’an and hadith where the aim is to clarify the speaker's intent. Thus definition, for Ibn Taymiyya, is an instrument of linguistic clarification and distinction, not of metaphysical discovery.

According to Ibn Taymiyya, what does 'ittiraad' (coextensiveness) mean?
A) The absence of the definition entails absence of the defined.

?
Test the definition 'al-khamr is every intoxicating drink' for ittiraad and in'ikaas. Is it coextensive with the class of khamr? Are there intoxicating drinks that are not khamr? Use the conditions to assess.
?
Consider the definition of 'tahara' (ritual purity) as 'removing impurity and performing wudu/ghusl'. Does this definition satisfy ittiraad and in'ikaas? What about someone who is in a state of purity without having removed impurity? Apply the test.

Methodological Role of Definition in Tafsir and Islamic Sciences
Ibn Taymiyya positions definition as translation and a religious task in tafsir.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the role of definition in Islamic exegesis and explain why it is considered a religious matter.

Core Idea
Definition in Islamic sciences is translation of meaning to clarify intent.

Ibn Taymiyya extends his linguistic theory of definition to explain its practical role in Islamic disciplines like tafsir and ghareeb al-Quran. According to him, definition serves as a translation from one expression to another, enabling us to understand the speaker's intent. This lesson explores that role and its significance for religious knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Understand definition as translation
Ibn Taymiyya explicitly states that the methodological role of definition is 'الترجمة بلفظ عن لفظ' (translation from one expression to another).
2
Connect to examples in tafsir
He says that explaining strange words (ghareeb) of the Qur’an and hadith, and even tafsir of the Qur’an in its first degrees, falls under this category.
The purpose is to mention the speaker's intent (مراد المتكلم).
3
Recognise the religious dimension
Ibn Taymiyya asserts that these definitions (hudud) are a matter of religion (معرفتها من الدين). This elevates the act of definition to a religious duty when it concerns the understanding of revelation.
4
Contrast with the metaphysical role
The definition does not prove the correctness of the speaker's speech (that requires a separate proof); it only clarifies the meaning. This keeps definition within the realm of language and interpretation.

Mere conception of the named thing, without judging that it is that sought or something else – we say: then it is like mere indication of a single word to its limited meaning, which is the indication of the name to its named. So the indication of the definition becomes like the indication of the name, and a mere name does not necessitate conception of the named for one who has not conceived it, without dispute. So the definition is a type of name, and if the name does not necessitate conception of the defined, then the definition also does not necessitate conception of the defined. Thus the purpose of definition according to Ibn Taymiyya – and arriving at the inner reality (kunn) or quiddity (mahiyya) was the purpose of definition among the Aristotelians – is distinguishing between the defined and others (1). As for conceiving the defined, the definition cannot do it as we said. But it may alert to the conception of the defined as the name alerts, for the mind may be heedless of a thing; when it hears its name alone, it turns its mind to the thing indicated by the name or the definition and conceives it, so the benefit of the definition is one of the benefits of the name (2). And if the benefit of the definition is distinguishing between the defined and others, then what is the law we use to capture the definition, how does this distinction occur? Ibn Taymiyya says: that occurs by the necessary description (al-wasf al-lazim) for definitions universally and exclusively (tardan wa ‘aksan), which entails from its affirmation the affirmation of the defined, and from its negation the negation of the defined, as is the method of the theoreticians (nuzzar) of the Muslims from all sects. And Ibn Taymiyya mentions that Abu al-Ma‘ali al-Juwayni says: the investigators among the theoreticians have agreed that coextensiveness (ittiraad) or counterextensiveness (in‘ikaas) are among its conditions. And Ibn Taymiyya holds that if the purpose of the definition is distinguishing the defined by its attribute from what is not it, then that is not achieved except with coextensiveness and counterextensiveness. Coextensiveness (tard) is the verification of the defined with the definition; counterextensiveness (‘aks) is the negation of the defined with the definition. Thus Ibn Taymiyya establishes his idea of definition on the issue of coextensiveness and counterextensiveness. For (definitions of species by attributes are like definitions of physical objects by directions, as when it is said: the boundary of the land from the southern side is such and such, and from the eastern side such and such; it distinguished the land by its name and its boundary. And the boundary of the land is needed if one fears increase or decrease of the named. It benefits by including all of the defined and excluding what is not of it, just as the name benefits. Likewise the boundary of the species. This relates sometimes to verbal definitions and sometimes to conventional definitions. The reality of definition in both places is only the clarification of the meaning of the name, and distinguishing the defined from others, not the conception of the defined (1). So coextensiveness and counterextensiveness lead to the clarification of the meaning of the name. And in this is the establishment of definition on a linguistic matter. (If the benefit of definition is clarification of the meaning of the name, and naming is a linguistic conventional matter that returns to the intention of that speaker and his language. Hence the jurists say: some names are known by their definition through the Law, and some by their definition through custom. From this is the interpretation (tafsir) of speech and its explanation; if what is intended is clarification of the speaker's intent, then it is built upon knowledge of the definitions of his speech; and if what is intended is clarification of its correctness and confirmation, then it needs knowledge of a proof of its correctness. The first involves clarification of the depiction of his speech. And the depiction of his speech is for depicting the meanings of the names by translation, sometimes for one who has already conceived the named but did not know that that is its name, and sometimes for one who has not conceived the named, so the named is pointed to as far as possible, either to its essence or to its likeness. Hence it is said: definition sometimes is for the name and sometimes for the named.) So the idea of conceiving the defined is not definition. Rather it is another process after definition, needing proofs to establish the correctness of the speaker's speech (2). 2. The methodological role of definition: The methodological role of definition is then 'translation from one expression to another. From this category is the mention of strange words of the Qur'an and hadith and others; rather the interpretation (tafsir) of the Qur'an and other types of speech is in its first degrees from this category, for the purpose is mentioning the speaker's intent by those names and by that speech.' Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage presents Ibn Taymiyya's critique of the Aristotelian theory of definition (hadd). The author first summarizes Ibn Taymiyya's argument that definition does not produce new conceptual knowledge (tasawwur) of the defined. Just as a mere name (ism) does not generate conception of the named for one who has no prior acquaintance, so too definition, being a type of name, cannot generate a new conception. Consequently, the purpose of definition is not to convey the essence (kunn or mahiyya) as the Aristotelians claimed, but only to distinguish the defined from other things. Ibn Taymiyya concedes that definition may alert the mind to a concept already possessed, but that is the same function as a name. Having established that definition serves only to distinguish, Ibn Taymiyya asks for the method (qanun) to capture a proper definition. He answers that it occurs through a necessary description (wasf lazim) that is both coextensive (tard) and counterextensive (‘aks), meaning that the description is present whenever the defined is present and absent whenever the defined is absent. This is the method of the Muslim theoreticians (nuzzar) of all schools. Ibn Taymiyya cites al-Juwayni's agreement that coextensiveness and counterextensiveness are conditions for a correct definition. Ibn Taymiyya clarifies further: coextensiveness (ittiraad) means that the defined is always accompanied by the definition; counterextensiveness (in‘ikaas) means that absence of the definition entails absence of the defined. He then draws an analogy: just as the physical boundary of a plot of land distinguishes it by directions, so the definition of a species distinguishes it by attributes. The definition, like the name, serves to include all instances of the defined and exclude everything else. The author then explains that this approach grounds definition in a linguistic (lughawi) framework. The benefit of definition is to clarify the meaning of a name (misma al-ism). Since naming is a conventional linguistic act, definition becomes a matter of translation: either translating a name for someone who knows the thing but not the word, or pointing to the thing itself or its likeness for someone unfamiliar with the thing. Hence definition is of two types: definition of the name (hadd al-ism) and definition of the named (hadd al-musamma). Conception of the defined (tasawwur al-mahdud) is not the role of definition; it requires a separate demonstration (dalil) to prove the correctness of the speaker's speech. The passage concludes by stating the methodological role of definition: it is translation from one expression to another, applicable in fields like exegesis (tafsir) of the Qur’an and hadith where the aim is to clarify the speaker's intent. Thus definition, for Ibn Taymiyya, is an instrument of linguistic clarification and distinction, not of metaphysical discovery.

غريب القرآن

Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of Definition: Name vs. Essence
Author explains that definition is merely clarifying the name, not conveying the essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish Ibn Taymiyya's view of definition (hadd) as merely clarifying the name from the Aristotelian view of definition as conveying the essence.

Core Idea
Definition explains the name, not the essence.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya challenges the classical logical conception of definition. He argues that a definition does not give us conceptual knowledge (tasawwur) of the essence; it only tells us what a name means. This has profound implications for how we understand knowledge and argumentation in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central claim
Locate where the author states that the reality of definition is only an explanation of the meaning of the name (muskam) and distinguishing the defined from others, not conceptualization of the limited.
This claim is the core of Ibn Taymiyya's critique.
2
Distinguish verbal and conventional definitions
Note that the author says definitions can be verbal (lafzi) or conventional (wad'i). Both types serve only to clarify the name, not the essence.
This undermines the Aristotelian notion of a 'real definition'.
3
Understand the role of tard wa 'aks
The author uses the logical criteria of coextensiveness (tard) and mutual exclusion ('aks) to ensure that the definition includes all instances and excludes all non-instances. This supports the function of clarifying the name.
These criteria are borrowed from usul al-fiqh.
4
Recognize the separation of definition and conceptualization
The author explicitly states that conceptualization (tasawwur) of the defined is not a definition. It is a separate process that requires evidence to prove the correctness of the speaker's speech.
This redefines the scope of logic and epistemology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The eastern such, the earth was distinguished by its name and its limit. And the limit of the earth is needed if there is fear of increase or decrease in the named. So it benefits by including all of the limited and excluding what is not from it, just as the name does. Likewise the limit of the species. And this is connected to verbal definitions sometimes and to conventional ones at other times, and the reality of the definition in both positions is only an explanation of the meaning of the name, and distinguishing the limited from others, not conceptualization of the limited. So the coextensiveness and mutual exclusion therefore lead to explaining the meaning of the name. And in this is an establishment of definition on a linguistic matter (since the benefit of definition is explaining the meaning of the name, and naming is a linguistic conventional matter that refers back to the intention of the named and its language. And for this the jurists say: among names are those whose definition is known by Shari'ah and among them are those whose definition is known by custom. And from this is the interpretation of speech and its explanation. If by it is intended clarification of the speaker's intent, then this is built upon knowledge of the limits of his speech. And if by it is intended clarification of its correctness and establishment, then it needs knowledge of evidence for its correctness. The first contains an explanation of the depiction of his speech. And the depiction of his speech is for depicting the meanings of names by translation sometimes for one who has conceptualized the meaning but did not know that is its name, and sometimes for one who has not conceptualized the meaning, so one points to the meaning as much as possible, either to its essence or to its likeness. Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage discusses the nature of definition (hadd) from Ibn Taymiyya's perspective. The author begins by giving an example of defining 'earth' by its name, illustrating that the definition serves to include everything that falls under the term and exclude what does not, similar to the function of a name. He then generalizes to the definition of a species, noting that definitions can be verbal (lafzi) or conventional (wad'i). In both types, the true function of a definition is merely to explain the meaning of a name (muskam) and to distinguish the defined from other things, not to provide conceptual knowledge (tasawwur) of the essence. The author then introduces the concepts of tard wa 'aks (coextensiveness and mutual exclusion), which are tools used to clarify the meaning of a name. He states that this establishes definition on a linguistic basis, since naming is a conventional matter dependent on the intention of the speaker. The jurists recognize that some definitions are derived from Shari'ah and others from custom. The passage then discusses the interpretation of speech: if the goal is to understand the speaker's intended meaning, one must know the limits (definitions) of his words; if the goal is to verify the truth of the speech, one needs evidence. The first case involves depicting the speaker's speech through translation (tarjama) for someone who already knows the concept but not the name, or by pointing to the concept or its equivalent for someone who does not know it. The author concludes that the conceptualization of the defined is not itself a definition; it is a separate process that comes after definition and requires evidence to confirm the correctness of the speaker's statement.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya redefines definition as a linguistic tool that clarifies names, not a gateway to essences.

Ibn Taymiyya's Verbal Definition: Translation, Not Essence
Author explains that definition serves as translation between words and is essential in all sciences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze Ibn Taymiyya's concept of definition as translation and distinguish it from Aristotelian real definition.

Core Idea
Definition is translation of words, not capture of essences.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya shifts the purpose of definition from philosophical essence-hunting to practical communication. He grounds this in religious obligation: understanding Allah's words requires knowing definitions. This lesson unpacks his argument and its radical implications for how we approach all sciences, from fiqh to mathematics.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target doctrine
What exactly is Ibn Taymiyya rejecting? The passage implies a critique of Aristotelian real definition (al-hadd al-haqiqi) that aims to capture the essence or quiddity. He offers an alternative.
2
Extract his positive view
Find the core phrase: definition is 'translation from one word to another' (tarjama bi-lafz 'an lafz). This means clarifying unfamiliar terms by familiar ones, not revealing essences.
3
Analyze his evidence
Ibn Taymiyya supports his view by pointing to Quranic interpretation, hadith commentary, and the act of reading any book (grammar, medicine, fiqh, theology). In all these, the goal is to understand the speaker's intended meaning, not to define the essence of things.
4
Determine the religious obligation
He classifies knowledge of such definitions as either an individual obligation (fard 'ayn) or a collective obligation (fard kifaya), depending on the context. This shows definitions are not optional but necessary for religious understanding.
This juristic classification is usually applied to actions, but Ibn Taymiyya applies it to knowledge of definitions.
5
Compare with Western parallels
The author notes that Mill, Russell, and Johnson later adopted a similar nominalist view, rejecting real definitions as useless. This shows Ibn Taymiyya's critique was ahead of its time.

فالدور المنهجي للحد إذن هو « الترجمة بلفظ عن لفظ ومن هذا الباب ذكر غريب القرآن والحديث وغيرها : بل تفسير القرآن وغيره من أنواع الكلام هو في أول درجاته من هذا الباب ، فإن المقصود ذكر مراد المتكلم بتلك الأسماء وبذلك الكلام » ويذكر ابن تيمية ( أن هذه الحدود معرفتها من الدين في كل لفظ هو في كتاب الله تعالى وسنة رسوله صلى الله عليه وسلم . ثم قد تكون معرفتها فرض عين - وقد تكون فرض كفاية » وهذا الحد اللفظي « هو الذي يحتاج إليه في إقراء العلوم المصنفة بل في قراءة جميع الكتب ، بل في جميع أنواع المخاطبات . فإن من قرأ كتب النحو أو الطب أو غيرها لا بد أن يعرف مراد أصحابها بتلك الأسماء ويعرف مرادهم بالكلام المؤلف . وكذلك من قرأ كتب الفقه والكلام والفلسفة وغيره »(١) فالحد اللفظي إذن هو الوحيد الذي يستخدم في العلوم . عبر ابن تيمية في بساطة عن هذا الرأي أخذ به جمع كبير من مناطقة الإنجليز وبخاصة جون استيوارت مل فقد اعتبر مجرد شرح اللفظ(٢) . بل إننا نجد الاتجاه اللفظي في الحد لدى فيلسوف انجلترا المعاصر برتراند رسل Russel . لم يقبل رسل الحد الأرسططاليسي التام ، لأن التعريف الفلسفي الذي يتجه نحو تفهم الماهية أو الفكر في جزئياتها إلا فائدة منه عنده . ثم يقول إنه سيهمل هذا التعريف الفلسفي وسيأخذ في دراسته الرياضية بالتعريف اللفظي البحت(۳) وهذا ما يردده Johnson(٤) .
The methodological role of definition is: 'Translation from one word to another, and from this is the mention of unusual words of the Quran and hadith and others; rather the interpretation of the Quran and other types of speech is in its first stages from this, because the objective is to mention the intention of the speaker with those names and with that speech.' And Ibn Taymiyya mentions: 'That these definitions, knowledge of them is from the religion in every word that is in the Book of Allah and the Sunnah of His Messenger. Then knowledge of them may be an individual obligation or a collective obligation.' And this verbal definition 'is that which is needed in teaching the classified sciences, rather in reading all books, rather in all types of communications. For whoever reads books of grammar or medicine or others, he must know the intention of their authors with those names and know their intention with the composed speech. And likewise whoever reads books of fiqh, theology, philosophy, and others.' So the verbal definition is the only one used in the sciences. Ibn Taymiyya simply expressed this opinion, adopted by a large group of English logicians especially John Stuart Mill, for he considered it merely an explanation of the word. Indeed, we find the verbal tendency in definition with the contemporary English philosopher Bertrand Russell. Russell did not accept the complete Aristotelian definition, because the philosophical definition that tends towards understanding the quiddity or thought in its particulars has no benefit according to him. Then he says he will neglect this philosophical definition and will adopt in his mathematical study the purely verbal definition. And this is repeated by Johnson.
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage presents the methodological role of definition according to Ibn Taymiyya. The author begins by quoting Ibn Taymiyya that definition is essentially a translation from one word to another, a process used in explaining unusual words in the Quran and Hadith, and even in Quranic interpretation. The purpose is to clarify the speaker's intended meaning. Ibn Taymiyya then states that knowledge of these definitions is part of religion, and it can be an individual or collective obligation. He emphasizes that verbal definition is necessary for reading any book—whether in grammar, medicine, fiqh, theology, or philosophy—because one must understand the author's intended meanings. The author concludes that verbal definition is the only type used in the sciences. The passage then draws a parallel between Ibn Taymiyya's view and modern English logicians such as John Stuart Mill, who also considered definition merely as explanation of words, and Bertrand Russell, who rejected Aristotelian real definitions as useless and adopted purely verbal definitions in mathematics. The author notes that this view is echoed by Johnson. Thus, the passage positions Ibn Taymiyya's approach as a precursor to modern nominalism.

Key Terms

ترجمة بلفظ عن لفظ
فرض عين
فرض كفاية

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya redefines definition as translation of words, making it an essential tool for understanding Islamic texts and all sciences.

Sources of Ibn Taymiyya's Theory of Definition
Author identifies the theological (kalami) definition as the main source for Ibn Taymiyya's theory.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the primary source of Ibn Taymiyya's theory of definition and explain why his approach differs from Aristotelian logic.

Core Idea
Ibn Taymiyya’s definition theory comes from Islamic theology, not Greek philosophy.

This passage reveals the intellectual roots of Ibn Taymiyya’s concept of definition. The author tells us that the most important source is the theological (kalami) definition, and that Ibn Taymiyya repeatedly cites the legal theorists (usuliyyun) and theologians (mutakallimun) while explicitly distancing himself from Aristotle. Understanding this provenance is crucial for evaluating his critique of Aristotelian logic.

Verbal vs. Real Definition in Ibn Taymiyya
Ibn Taymiyya's argument for verbal definition over essential definition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the verbal definition (al-hadd al-lafzi) and the Aristotelian real definition as presented by Ibn Taymiyya.

Core Idea
Ibn Taymiyya rejects essential definitions, accepting only verbal definitions as practical and sufficient for sciences.

In this passage, the author explains Ibn Taymiyya's radical departure from the Aristotelian tradition. Ibn Taymiyya argues that in reading any book—be it grammar, medicine, or fiqh—what matters is understanding the author's intended meaning of words, not their essences. This leads him to accept only verbal definitions as practical and sufficient. You will learn to identify and compare these two types of definitions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of definition
Locate the terms 'الحد اللفظي' (verbal definition) and 'الحد الأرسططاليسي' (Aristotelian definition) in the Arabic. Notice how each is described: verbal definition is about knowing the author's intent; Aristotelian aims to capture essence (mahiyya).
The passage contrasts them explicitly.
2
Understand Ibn Taymiyya's rejection of Aristotelian definition
Ibn Taymiyya claims that only verbal definitions are used in sciences. He criticizes the Aristotelian definition as unnecessary for actual scholarly practice. Find the phrases that show this rejection.
The author mentions that Russell and Mill later held similar views, but focus on Ibn Taymiyya's own argument.
3
Apply the distinction to an Islamic science
Consider how a jurist defines 'wine' (khamr) in fiqh: a verbal definition would be 'the intoxicating beverage made from grapes,' while a real definition would be 'the fermented juice of grapes that causes intoxication.' Which one does Ibn Taymiyya consider useful? Why?
Think about which definition helps in practical legal reasoning.

Every word is in the Book of God and the Sunnah of His Messenger, peace be upon him. Then, knowing them may be an individual obligation or a collective obligation. This verbal definition is what is needed in teaching the classified sciences, nay, in reading all books, nay, in all types of discourses. For whoever reads books of grammar, medicine, or others, must know the authors' intent with those names and know their intent with the composed speech. Likewise, whoever reads books of jurisprudence, theology, philosophy, and others. So the verbal definition is the only one used in the sciences. Ibn Taymiyya expressed this opinion simply; a large group of English logicians adopted it, especially John Stuart Mill, who considered it merely explaining the word. Indeed, we find the verbal trend in definition in the contemporary English philosopher Bertrand Russell. Russell did not accept the complete Aristotelian definition, because the philosophical definition that tends towards understanding the essence or thought in its particulars has no benefit in his view. Then he says he will disregard this philosophical definition and take in his mathematical study the pure verbal definition; this is echoed by Johnson. 3. Sources of Ibn Taymiyya's topic of definition: The most important source of this research is the theological definition. Ibn Taymiyya frequently refers to the discussions of definition among the usuliyyun and mutakallimun, and transmits much of their speech and opinions. He repeats in multiple passages that they did not adopt the Aristotelian topic of definition. Ibn Taymiyya's thought on definition approaches that of the mutakallimun greatly. But did Stoic logic influence Ibn Taymiyya? We cannot assert or incline towards this - firstly, because we do not have the original sources of this Stoic logic to decide whether the Muslims in their own logic were influenced by the Stoic investigations. Secondly, the disagreement among historians of philosophy in Europe now regarding the reality of this logic, especially concerning definition: Did the Stoics establish a new theory of definition, or did they follow Aristotle? Brochard takes the first view; Hamelin and with him Prantl and Zeller take the second. So the issues of Stoic logic are still pending. This leads us to say that Ibn Taymiyya's treatment of definition is mostly an innovative work at least. Ibn Taymiyya thus criticized the Aristotelian definition with non-Aristotelian Greek elements on one hand, and with elements he innovated on the other, but he established a treatment of definition that can be considered new. We will follow in the next chapter the study of Ibn Taymiyya's critique of the Aristotelian theory of the proposition.
Source pages p. 203

Commentary
This passage is a scholarly analysis of Ibn Taymiyya's approach to definition (hadd). It opens by stating that every word in the Quran and Sunnah may be subject to an individual or collective obligation to understand, and that only verbal definitions (al-hadd al-lafzi) are used in the sciences. The author explains that Ibn Taymiyya held this view, which was later adopted by English logicians like John Stuart Mill and Bertrand Russell, who rejected Aristotelian essential definitions in favor of pure verbal ones. The passage then turns to the sources of Ibn Taymiyya's thought on definition. It identifies the theological (kalami) definition as the primary source, noting that Ibn Taymiyya frequently references the debates of usuliyyun and mutakallimun, who did not adopt Aristotelian definitions. The author then considers the possible influence of Stoic logic, but acknowledges uncertainty due to lack of original sources and scholarly disagreement among historians. Ultimately, the passage concludes that Ibn Taymiyya's theory of definition is largely innovative, combining non-Aristotelian Greek elements with his own contributions. It ends by previewing the next chapter on Ibn Taymiyya's critique of Aristotelian propositions.

Sources of Ibn Taymiyya's Theory of Definition
Tracing the sources of Ibn Taymiyya's theory of definition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the main sources of Ibn Taymiyya's theory of definition as discussed by the author: the kalam tradition, possibly Stoic logic, and his own innovation.

Core Idea
Ibn Taymiyya's definition theory is rooted in Islamic theology (kalam) and is largely innovative, despite possible Stoic influences.

The passage explores where Ibn Taymiyya got his ideas about definition. The author points to the theological (kalami) tradition as the primary source, noting that Ibn Taymiyya frequently references mutakallimun and usuliyyun. The possibility of Stoic influence is also considered but left unresolved. This lesson will help you trace these influences and understand the innovative nature of Ibn Taymiyya's work.

Guided Path
1
Identify the primary source mentioned
The author states that the 'most important source' is 'الحد الكلامي' (theological definition). Look for phrases where Ibn Taymiyya references the mutakallimun and usuliyyun.
This is contrasted with the Aristotelian tradition.
2
Examine the discussion of Stoic influence

3

في كل لفظ هو في كتاب الله تعالى وسنة رسوله صلى الله عليه وسلم . ثم قد تكون معرفتها فرض عين - وقد تكون فرض كفاية » وهذا الحد اللفظي « هو الذي يحتاج إليه في إقراء العلوم المصنفة بل في قراءة جميع الكتب ، بل في جميع أنواع المخاطبات . فإن من قرأ كتب النحو أو الطب أو غيرها لا بد أن يعرف مراد أصحابها بتلك الأسماء ويعرف مرادهم بالكلام المؤلف . وكذلك من قرأ كتب الفقه والكلام والفلسفة وغيره »(۱) فالحد اللفظي إذن هو الوحيد الذي يستخدم في العلوم . عبر ابن تيمية في بساطة عن هذا الرأي أخذ به جمع كبير من مناطقة الإنجليز وبخاصة جون استيوارت مل فقد اعتبر مجرد شرح اللفظ(٢) . بل إننا نجد الاتجاه اللفظي في الحد لدى فيلسوف انجلترا المعاصر برتراند رسل Russel . لم يقبل رسل الحد الأرسططاليسي التام ، لأن التعريف الفلسفي الذي يتجه نحو تفهم الماهية أو الفكر في جزئياتها إلا فائدة منه عنده . ثم يقول إنه سيهمل هذا التعريف الفلسفي وسيأخذ في دراسته الرياضية بالتعريف اللفظي البحت(۳) وهذا ما يردده Johnson(٤) . ٣ - مصادر مبحث الحد التيمي : أهم مصادر هذا البحث هو الحد الكلامي . وابن تيمية يشير إلى مباحث الحد عند الأصوليين والمتكلمين كثيراً وينقل لنا كثيراً من كلامهم وآرائهم . ويكرر في فقرات متعددة أنهم لم يأخذوا بمبحث الحد الأرسططاليسي . ويقترب فكر ابن تيمية في الحد من فكر المتكلمين كثيراً . ولكن هل أثر المنطق الرواقي على ابن تيمية - لا نستطيع أن نجزم أو نرجح بهذا - أولاً - لأنه ليس بين أيدينا مصادر هذا المنطق الرواقية الأصلية حتى يمكننا أن نقرر هل تأثر المسلمون في منطقهم الخاص بمباحث الرواقيين . ثانياً ـ اختلاف مؤرخي الفلسفة في أوروبا الآن حول حقيقة هذا المنطق ، وبخاصة في مبحث الحد : هل وضع الرواقيون نظرية في الحد جديدة أم أنهم تابعوا أرسطو ؟ يأخذ بروشارد Brochard بالرأي الأول ويأخذ هاملان Hamelin ومعه في هذا برانتل Prantel وتسلم Zeller بالرأي الثاني فمسائل المنطق الرواقي ما زالت معلقة . وهذا ما يجعلنا نقول إن مبحث الحد التيمي هو عمل ابتكاري في معظمه على الأقل . فابن تيمية إذن نقد الحد الأرسططاليسي بعناصر غير أرسططاليسية يونانية من ناحية ، وعناصر ابتدعها من ناحية أخرى ولكنه وضع مبحثاً في الحد يمكن اعتباره جديداً . . . وسنتابع في الفصل القادم بحث نقد ابن تيمية لمبحث القضية الأرسططاليسية .
Every word is in the Book of God and the Sunnah of His Messenger, peace be upon him. Then, knowing them may be an individual obligation or a collective obligation. This verbal definition is what is needed in teaching the classified sciences, nay, in reading all books, nay, in all types of discourses. For whoever reads books of grammar, medicine, or others, must know the authors' intent with those names and know their intent with the composed speech. Likewise, whoever reads books of jurisprudence, theology, philosophy, and others. So the verbal definition is the only one used in the sciences. Ibn Taymiyya expressed this opinion simply; a large group of English logicians adopted it, especially John Stuart Mill, who considered it merely explaining the word. Indeed, we find the verbal trend in definition in the contemporary English philosopher Bertrand Russell. Russell did not accept the complete Aristotelian definition, because the philosophical definition that tends towards understanding the essence or thought in its particulars has no benefit in his view. Then he says he will disregard this philosophical definition and take in his mathematical study the pure verbal definition; this is echoed by Johnson. 3. Sources of Ibn Taymiyya's topic of definition: The most important source of this research is the theological definition. Ibn Taymiyya frequently refers to the discussions of definition among the usuliyyun and mutakallimun, and transmits much of their speech and opinions. He repeats in multiple passages that they did not adopt the Aristotelian topic of definition. Ibn Taymiyya's thought on definition approaches that of the mutakallimun greatly. But did Stoic logic influence Ibn Taymiyya? We cannot assert or incline towards this - firstly, because we do not have the original sources of this Stoic logic to decide whether the Muslims in their own logic were influenced by the Stoic investigations. Secondly, the disagreement among historians of philosophy in Europe now regarding the reality of this logic, especially concerning definition: Did the Stoics establish a new theory of definition, or did they follow Aristotle? Brochard takes the first view; Hamelin and with him Prantl and Zeller take the second. So the issues of Stoic logic are still pending. This leads us to say that Ibn Taymiyya's treatment of definition is mostly an innovative work at least. Ibn Taymiyya thus criticized the Aristotelian definition with non-Aristotelian Greek elements on one hand, and with elements he innovated on the other, but he established a treatment of definition that can be considered new. We will follow in the next chapter the study of Ibn Taymiyya's critique of the Aristotelian theory of the proposition.
Source pages p. 203

Commentary
This passage is a scholarly analysis of Ibn Taymiyya's approach to definition (hadd). It opens by stating that every word in the Quran and Sunnah may be subject to an individual or collective obligation to understand, and that only verbal definitions (al-hadd al-lafzi) are used in the sciences. The author explains that Ibn Taymiyya held this view, which was later adopted by English logicians like John Stuart Mill and Bertrand Russell, who rejected Aristotelian essential definitions in favor of pure verbal ones. The passage then turns to the sources of Ibn Taymiyya's thought on definition. It identifies the theological (kalami) definition as the primary source, noting that Ibn Taymiyya frequently references the debates of usuliyyun and mutakallimun, who did not adopt Aristotelian definitions. The author then considers the possible influence of Stoic logic, but acknowledges uncertainty due to lack of original sources and scholarly disagreement among historians. Ultimately, the passage concludes that Ibn Taymiyya's theory of definition is largely innovative, combining non-Aristotelian Greek elements with his own contributions. It ends by previewing the next chapter on Ibn Taymiyya's critique of Aristotelian propositions.

Key Terms
الحد الكلامي
المنطق الرواقي
المتكلمين

D) English empiricism

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya's theory of definition draws primarily from the kalam tradition and is largely innovative, with uncertain Stoic influence.

Innovation in Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of Aristotelian Definition
The innovative nature of Ibn Taymiyya's definition theory

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate Ibn Taymiyya's contribution to definition theory as an innovative synthesis, based on the author's analysis.

Core Idea
Ibn Taymiyya's critique of Aristotelian definition is an innovative blend of non-Aristotelian Greek elements and his own ideas.

The passage concludes that Ibn Taymiyya's treatment of definition is 'mostly innovative'. He used non-Aristotelian Greek elements (possibly from kalam or other sources) and also introduced his own innovations. This lesson focuses on understanding how Ibn Taymiyya's critique led to a new approach in definition theory.

Guided Path
1
Identify what Ibn Taymiyya criticized
Find the specific Aristotelian definition theory that Ibn Taymiyya rejected. The passage mentions 'الحد الأرسططاليسي التام' (complete Aristotelian definition).
This definition aims at essence (mahiyya) through genus and differentia.
2
Recognize the non-Aristotelian elements
The author says Ibn Taymiyya used 'non-Aristotelian Greek elements' ('عناصر غير أرسططاليسية يونانية'). Think of the kalami tradition as one such source.
These elements might include a focus on verbal usage rather than essence.
3
Understand the innovative aspect
Ibn Taymiyya also added his own innovations ('عناصر ابتدعها'). Consider what new elements he introduced, such as the emphasis on the practical communicative function of definition.
The passage suggests his theory is new ('جديد').

The Unity of the Logical Demand: Affirmation or Denial
Ibn Taymiyya concludes that the demand in reasoning is one thing: affirmation or denial of a predicate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the single demand (matlub) in any proposition as either affirmation or denial of a predicate, and explain Ibn Taymiyya's argument for its unity.

Core Idea
Every rational investigation seeks one thing: is the predicate true of the subject or not?

Ibn Taymiyya cuts through the apparent multiplicity of logical demands. Whether you ask 'Is nabidh haram?' or 'Is man an animal?', the underlying question is always the same: does the predicate attach to the subject? In this passage, he establishes the foundational unity of the matlub, which shapes how we evaluate arguments and definitions in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim
Find Ibn Taymiyya's thesis: the matlub is one thing—either thubut (affirmation) or intifa' (negation) of the judgmental relation.
2
Examine the examples
Look at the Arabic examples: 'Nabidh is forbidden' and 'Man is an animal'. For each, note that the matlub is the thubut or intifa' of the predicate (forbiddance, animality).
3
Understand the terminology
Define 'thubut' as the predicate's positive connection to the subject, and 'intifa'' as its negation. Recognize that 'nisba hukmiyya' (judgmental relation) is the core.
4
Apply the concept
Given any proposition in Islamic sciences, ask: does it affirm or deny the predicate of the subject? That single demand is the matlub.
Practice with: 'Salat is obligatory' → thubut of obligation; 'Wine is not permissible' → intifa' of permissibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويخلص ابن تيمية إلى القول بأن المطلوب في العقل ليس شيئين في الواقع ، بل شيء واحد : هو ثبوت النسبة الحكمية أو انتفاؤها ، أو اتصال الموصوف بالصفة نفياً أو إثباتاً ، أو نسبة المحمول إلى الموضوع ، والخبر إلى المبدأ نفياً وإثباتاً . فإذا ما قلنا النبيذ حرام أو ليس بحرام أو الإنسان حيوان أو ليس بحيوان ، كان المطلوب ثبوت التحريم للنبيذ أو انتفاؤه . وكذلك ثبوت الحس للإنسان أو انتفاؤه . فالمطلوب في العقل إذن شيء واحد(١) .
Ibn Taymiyya concludes that the demand (al-matlub) in the intellect is not two things in reality, but one thing: it is the affirmation of the judgmental relation or its negation, or the connection of the described thing to the attribute negatively or affirmatively, or the predication of the predicate to the subject, and the report to the subject negatively or affirmatively. So if we say 'Nabidh is forbidden' or 'is not forbidden', or 'Man is an animal' or 'is not an animal', the demand is the affirmation of forbiddance for nabidh or its negation. Likewise, the affirmation of sensation for man or its negation. So the demand in the intellect is thus one thing.
Source pages p. 234

Commentary
This passage presents Ibn Taymiyya's conclusion regarding the nature of the demand (matlub) in rational inquiry. He argues that the matlub is always a single thing: either the affirmation or denial of a predicate of a subject. He equates this with the judgmental relation, the connection of attribute to substance, or the predication. Using Arabic examples, he shows that saying 'Nabidh is haram' or 'Nabidh is not haram' both aim at establishing either thubut (affirmation) or intifa' (negation) of the ruling (hukm). Similarly, 'Man is an animal' or 'Man is not an animal' seeks to establish the genus's presence or absence. Thus, despite appearances, the core logical demand is unitary—it is always about a single relational fact.

المطلوب
النسبة الحكمية
ثبوت
انتفاء

Ibn Taymiyya: Definitions Require Proof
Ibn Taymiyya argues definitions require proof and must align with Sharia.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's argument that definitions require proof and explain why he insists on a Sharia-based proof for defining religious terms.

Core Idea
Definitions alone yield no knowledge; only proof from revelation confirms them.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya concludes his critique of the logicians' view that definitions produce certain knowledge. He sides with early Muslim theologians who insisted that simple concepts are not known by definition alone. Instead, what we seek from a definition is an assent that itself requires proof, just like any other assent. For Ibn Taymiyya, this proof must ultimately come from the Sharia, as he illustrates with the examples of khamr and ghiba.

Guided Path
1
Identify Ibn Taymiyya's thesis
Determine his main claim about definitions. He states that simple concepts are not known by definition alone; definitions require proof.
2
Locate his historical authority
Who does he cite as being correct? The early Muslim theologians (nuzzar al-muslimin) up to the fifth century AH.
3
Trace the analogy between definitions and names
How does Ibn Taymiyya compare definitions to names? Definitions are detailings of what the name indicates in summary, and their purpose is distinction.
4
Examine the examples
What examples does he give (khamr, ghiba) and what point do they illustrate? The definer must provide a sharia proof that the definition matches the revealed usage.
5
Determine the implication
What is the role of sharia in defining religious terms? The definer must resort to a proof from the Lawgiver; definitions are not self-sufficient.

وينتهي ابن تيمية الى القول بأن نظار المسلمين ـ أي المتكلمين الأوائل حتى القرن الخامس الهجري ـ كانوا على حق حين قرروا أن التصورات المفردة لا تعلم بمجرد الحد . إن المطلوب بالحد هو تصديق يفتقر إلى ما تفتقر اليه التصديقات ، أي يفتقر إلى دليل . كما تفتقر التصديقات . وكذلك القياس انما يعود في الحقيقة إلى الحد والتصور ، كما يعود هذا القياس الى أن يعلم مسمى الخمر . « وإذا كان كذلك ، فإذا كانوا يقولون إن الحد لا يقام عليه دليل ، ولا يحتاج الى قيام دليل ، فنقول العلم بمسمى الخمر لا يحتاج الى قياس ، بل قد يعلم بما يعلم به سائر التصورات المفردة ومسميات جميع الأسماء من تفطن النفس لشمول ذلك المعنى لهذه الصورة وثبوته فيها » ولذلك أصاب المتكلمون الأوائل حين قرروا أن الحدود بمنزلة الأسماء ، أي أنها تفصيل ما دل عليه الإسم بالإجمال وأن ما نطلبه من الحد : هو التمييز بين المحدود وغيره ، وذلك يكون بالوصف الملازم له طرداً وعكساً ، بحيث يكون الحد جامعاً مانعاً . على أن لا يحد الحاد إلا بدليل . وهذا هو الشرط الذي ينبغي أن يتوفر في الحاد . ويعطي ابن تيمية أمثلة لهذا . ان الحاد يقول في الخمر : إنها المسكر . وفي الغيبة : انها ذكرك أخاك بما لا يكره . فعلى الحاد أن يبين أن ما ذكره مطابق لمسمى ذلك الإسم . ويرى ابن تيمية أنه ينبغي أن يلجأ هنا إلى دليل من الشرع . فينقل عن الشارع المتكلم بهذه الاسماء ، فقد ثبت عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أنه قال « كل مسكر ...
Ibn Taymiyya concludes that the Muslim speculative theologians — that is, the early theologians up to the fifth Islamic century — were correct when they established that simple concepts are not known by definition alone. What is sought by definition is an assent that needs what other assents need, i.e., it needs a proof. Just as assents need proof. Similarly, syllogism in reality returns to definition and conception, just as this syllogism returns to knowing the meaning of khamr. 'If that is so, then if they say that definition is not established by proof and does not need a proof, we say: knowledge of the meaning of khamr does not need a syllogism, but rather it is known by what all other simple concepts and meanings of all names are known: by the mind's awareness of the inclusion of that meaning in this form and its establishment in it.' Therefore, the early theologians were correct when they established that definitions are like names: they are a detailing of what the name indicates in summary, and what we seek from definition is the distinction between the defined and other things, and that is by the description that is concomitant with it positively and negatively, such that the definition is comprehensive and exclusive. This requires that the definer must not define except with a proof. And this is the condition that should be fulfilled in the definer. Ibn Taymiyya gives examples of this. The definer says about khamr: it is the intoxicant. And about ghiba (backbiting): it is your mentioning your brother with something he dislikes. So the definer must show that what he mentioned corresponds to the meaning of that name. Ibn Taymiyya holds that one must resort here to a proof from the Sharia. For he quotes from the Lawgiver who spoke these names, as it is established from the Prophet (peace be upon him) that he said: 'Every intoxicant...'
Source pages p. 262

Commentary
This passage presents Ibn Taymiyya's final position on definitions (hudud). He sides with the early mutakallimun who held that simple concepts are not learned through definitions alone. Definitions are a form of assent that requires proof, just like any other assent. He connects this to his critique of the logicians' claim that syllogism produces new knowledge; even syllogism relies on prior knowledge of terms. He uses the example of khamr (wine): knowing its meaning does not require syllogism but rather mental awareness. Definitions are akin to names—they clarify what a name summarizes. The purpose of a definition is to distinguish the defined from others, using a description that co-extends with it (ta'rif bi al-rasm). The definer must provide a proof (dalil) from the Sharia, as in the case of khamr (intoxicant) or ghiba (backbiting). Ibn Taymiyya insists that the definer cannot rely on mere stipulation; he must demonstrate correspondence to the revealed name. This highlight's his epistemological commitment to revelation as the source of certain knowledge.

نظار المسلمين
جامع مانع

B) A proof from the Sharia showing the definition matches the revealed usage.
C) A syllogistic argument.
D) Nothing; definitions are self-evident.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya's position is that definitions of religious terms require proof from revelation; they are not self-evident.

Using Ibn Taymiyya's criterion, evaluate whether the definition 'riba is any excess in a loan' requires a Sharia proof. What would such a proof look like?

Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of Greek Definition Theory
Ibn Taymiyya argues that definition requires proof and rejects the Greek division of attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state Ibn Taymiyya's claim that every definition requires proof and contrast it with the Greek position that definition conveys quiddity without proof.

Core Idea
No definition without evidence—Ibn Taymiyya dismantles the Greek presumption of unmediated conception.

Ibn Taymiyya opens his critique by asserting that definition (hadd) cannot stand without a proof (dalil). He illustrates this with the consensus-based identification of wine as intoxicant, showing that definitional knowledge is derived from evidence, not from mere utterance. This directly challenges the Greek logicians, who held that a definition directly imparts the essence (mahiyya) to the listener's mind without any supporting argument. In this lesson, you will examine Ibn Taymiyya's foundational principle and its implications for how we understand definitions in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core claim
Extract Ibn Taymiyya's statement: 'There must therefore be a proof in the definition, whether it is a definition of a name or a definition according to reality.' This is the thesis he defends.
The claim is absolute: all definitions require evidence.
2
Analyse the example
Ibn Taymiyya uses the case of wine (khamr): 'What is not an intoxicant is not wine by consensus, so it is established that wine is the intoxicant.' Notice how he grounds the definition in consensus (ijma) as proof.
The example is drawn from fiqh, showing the practical necessity of evidence.
3
Contrast with the Greek position
He says: 'Not as the Greek logicians hold, that the definition conveys the conception of the quiddity in the mind of the listener and that this occurs merely by the statement of the definer without any proof at all.' Summarize the difference.
For Greeks, definition is a direct cognitive transfer; for Ibn Taymiyya, it requires external justification.
4
Distinguish types of definition
Ibn Taymiyya mentions two types: hadd ism (definition of a name) and hadd haqiqa (definition of reality). Both require proof. Consider how the need for proof differs between them.
A nominal definition may rely on linguistic convention, but still needs evidence to link name to thing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
خمر » ، وما ليس بمسكر فليس بخمر بالإجماع ، فيثبت أن الخمر هو المسكر . . . وكذلك في الغيبة . لا بد اذن في الحد من دليل ، سواء أكان حد اسم أو حداً بحسب الحقيقة . لا ما يذهب اليه المناطقة اليونانيون من أن الحد يفيد تصور الماهية في نفس المستمع وأن ذلك يحصل بمجرد قول الحاد بدون دليل أصلاً . وتقسيمهم الصفات اللازمة للموصوف إلى ثلاثة أصناف . ذاتية داخلة في الماهية ، وخارجة لازمة للماهية دون وجودها ، وخارجة لازمة لوجودها . وهذه كلها باطلة كما بين من قبل ، إذا أريد بالماهية الموجودات الخارجية . وأما إذا أرادوا بالماهية ما يتصور في النفس ، فقدر تلك الماهية وصفها يتبع تصور المتصور . فاذا أراد تصوير ماهية الإنسان في النفس اختلف التصوير باختلاف المتصور ، فتارة يتصور ماهية الإنسان جسماً حساساً نامياً متحركاً بالإرادة ناطقاً ، ويتصور حيواناً ناطقاً ، وتارة يتصور حيواناً ضاحكاً أو ضاحكاً فقط . قد يكون كل هذا صحيحاً في ذاته ، ولكن لا فائدة علمية منه ، ولا يصل أبداً إلى الحقيقة الموضوعية ، انه يعود فقط إلى تصور مراد المتكلم ، سواء كان حقاً أو باطلاً . ولذلك لم يأبه نظار المسلمين بقواعد التحديد المنطقية ، إنها في رأيهم آلية ميكانيكية لا تصل إلى شيء . ان الحد عندهم يكون بالوصف الواحد الملازم ، والوصف الواحد الملازم كاف فلا(۱) يذكرون الأجناس والفصول ولا العرض العام ، ولا يستخدمونها . (۱) ابن تيمية : الرد . . ص ٣٦١ .
Wine, and what is not an intoxicant is not wine by consensus, so it is established that wine is the intoxicant... And similarly in backbiting. There must therefore be a proof in the definition, whether it is a definition of a name or a definition according to reality. Not as the Greek logicians hold, that the definition conveys the conception of the quiddity in the mind of the listener and that this occurs merely by the statement of the definer without any proof at all. And their division of necessary attributes of the described into three kinds: essential attributes entering into the quiddity, external attributes necessary to the quiddity without its existence, and external attributes necessary to its existence. All of this is false as was shown before, if by quiddity they mean external existents. But if they mean by quiddity what is conceived in the mind, then the measure of that quiddity and its attribute follows the conception of the one who conceives. So if he wants to depict the quiddity of 'human' in the mind, the depiction differs according to the one who conceives: sometimes he conceives the quiddity of human as a sentient, growing, moving-by-will, speaking body; sometimes he conceives it as a rational animal; and sometimes he conceives it as a laughing animal or just laughing. All of this may be correct in itself, but it has no scientific benefit, and it never reaches objective truth; it merely returns to the conception of the speaker's intention, whether it is true or false. Therefore the Islamic scholars paid no attention to the logical rules of definition; in their view they are a mechanical device that achieves nothing. Indeed, definition for them is by a single necessary attribute, and a single necessary attribute suffices, so they do not mention genera, differentiae, or accidental universals, and they do not use them. (1) Ibn Taymiyya: al-Radd... p. 361.
Source pages p. 264

Commentary
This passage presents Ibn Taymiyya's critique of the Greek theory of definition (hadd). The author begins by illustrating a definitional argument: 'Wine is intoxicating' is established by consensus, showing that definition requires proof. Ibn Taymiyya then states categorically that every definition—whether nominal or real—must be supported by evidence, contrary to the Greek logicians who claim that a definition directly conveys the quiddity (mahiyya) to the listener's mind without any proof. Next, he attacks their division of necessary attributes into three categories: (1) essential attributes that are part of the quiddity, (2) external attributes necessary to the quiddity but not to its existence, and (3) external attributes necessary to its existence. He declares this classification false when applied to external existents. However, if by 'quiddity' they mean a mental concept, then the depiction varies according to the imaginer. For example, one may conceive 'human' as a rational animal or as a laughing animal; all these depictions may be correct subjectively but have no scientific value or objective truth. Consequently, Islamic scholars ignored these logical rules, considering them a sterile mechanism. Rather, they defined by a single necessary attribute (al-wasf al-wahid al-mulazim), without resorting to genera, differentiae, or accidental universals. This critique underscores Ibn Taymiyya's rejection of the epistemological pretensions of Aristotelian logic.

الحد
الماهية
دليل

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya insists that every definition, whether nominal or real, must be backed by proof, rejecting the Greek notion of direct essence-conveyance.

Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of Greek Definition Theory
Ibn Taymiyya argues that definition requires proof and rejects the Greek division of attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain Ibn Taymiyya's rejection of the Greek threefold division of necessary attributes and identify his argument that such divisions are either false or subjective.

Core Idea
Dividing attributes into essential and accidental is useless when quiddity is mental and subjective.

After establishing that definition requires proof, Ibn Taymiyya attacks the Greek classification of necessary attributes (al-sifāt al-lāzima) into three categories. He argues that if 'quiddity' refers to external existents, the division is simply false; if it refers to mental concepts, the division is subjective and varies with the individual. To illustrate, he gives the example of how one might define 'human' in different ways, all equally correct in the mind but none attaining objective truth. This lesson breaks down his critique and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Recall the three categories
The Greek division: (1) essential attributes part of the quiddity (dhatiyya), (2) external attributes necessary to quiddity but not existence, (3) external attributes necessary to existence. Identify each in the text.
These categories are standard in Aristotelian logic.
2
Analyse the first objection (external quiddity)
Ibn Taymiyya says: 'All of this is false... if by quiddity they mean external existents.' Why? Because in external reality, attributes are not separable in the way the division suggests.
He does not elaborate here but refers to a previous argument.
3
Analyse the second objection (mental quiddity)
If quiddity is a mental concept, then its attributes depend on the imaginer. The example: one person conceives human as 'rational animal', another as 'laughing animal'. Both are correct subjectively but not objectively.
The division becomes arbitrary; no objective classification is possible.
4
Evaluate the conclusion
Ibn Taymiyya concludes that such divisions have 'no scientific benefit' and 'never reach objective truth'. They only reflect the speaker's intention, regardless of truth.
This is a devastating critique of the epistemological value of the Greek taxonomy.

Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of Greek Definition Theory
Ibn Taymiyya argues that definition requires proof and rejects the Greek division of attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to describe the Islamic alternative to Greek definition: definition by a single necessary attribute (al-wasf al-wahid al-mulazim) and explain why genera and differentiae are unnecessary.

Core Idea
Islamic scholars define by one necessary attribute, not by hierarchical categories.

After his critique of the Greek approach, Ibn Taymiyya presents the positive method of Islamic scholars: definition through a single necessary attribute (al-wasf al-wahid al-mulazim). According to him, this single attribute suffices to identify the defined thing, rendering the elaborate machinery of genera, differentiae, and accidental universals superfluous. In this lesson, you will examine this alternative and understand why Ibn Taymiyya considers it superior for achieving practical, objective definitions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the alternative method
Ibn Taymiyya states: 'Indeed, definition for them is by a single necessary attribute, and a single necessary attribute suffices.' Find this statement in the text.
This is the key positive doctrine: definition = al-wasf al-wahid al-mulazim.
2
Understand why it suffices
A single necessary attribute (like 'intoxicating' for wine) uniquely picks out the thing. It does not require a full taxonomy. Reflect on the wine example: the attribute 'intoxicating' is both necessary and sufficient to define wine.
This method is simpler and more direct.
3
Contrast with Greek method
Ibn Taymiyya notes that Islamic scholars 'do not mention genera, differentiae, or accidental universals'. The Greek method uses a hierarchy (jins, fasl, 'arad 'amm). Why does Ibn Taymiyya reject these?
He views them as unnecessary complications that do not add certainty.
4
Evaluate the epistemological claim
Ibn Taymiyya asserts that the Greek rules are a 'mechanical device that achieves nothing'. The single-attribute method, by contrast, is directly tied to observable or known properties. Do you agree that this method avoids subjectivity?
Consider whether a single attribute can always capture the essence fully.

خمر » ، وما ليس بمسكر فليس بخمر بالإجماع ، فيثبت أن الخمر هو المسكر . . . وكذلك في الغيبة . لا بد اذن في الحد من دليل ، سواء أكان حد اسم أو حداً بحسب الحقيقة . لا ما يذهب اليه المناطقة اليونانيون من أن الحد يفيد تصور الماهية في نفس المستمع وأن ذلك يحصل بمجرد قول الحاد بدون دليل أصلاً . وتقسيمهم الصفات اللازمة للموصوف إلى ثلاثة أصناف . ذاتية داخلة في الماهية ، وخارجة لازمة للماهية دون وجودها ، وخارجة لازمة لوجودها . وهذه كلها باطلة كما بين من قبل ، إذا أريد بالماهية الموجودات الخارجية . وأما إذا أرادوا بالماهية ما يتصور في النفس ، فقدر تلك الماهية وصفها يتبع تصور المتصور . فاذا أراد تصوير ماهية الإنسان في النفس اختلف التصوير باختلاف المتصور ، فتارة يتصور ماهية الإنسان جسماً حساساً نامياً متحركاً بالإرادة ناطقاً ، ويتصور حيواناً ناطقاً ، وتارة يتصور حيواناً ضاحكاً أو ضاحكاً فقط . قد يكون كل هذا صحيحاً في ذاته ، ولكن لا فائدة علمية منه ، ولا يصل أبداً إلى الحقيقة الموضوعية ، انه يعود فقط إلى تصور مراد المتكلم ، سواء كان حقاً أو باطلاً . ولذلك لم يأبه نظار المسلمين بقواعد التحديد المنطقية ، إنها في رأيهم آلية ميكانيكية لا تصل إلى شيء . ان الحد عندهم يكون بالوصف الواحد الملازم ، والوصف الواحد الملازم كاف فلا(۱) يذكرون الأجناس والفصول ولا العرض العام ، ولا يستخدمونها . (۱) ابن تيمية : الرد . . ص ٣٦١ .
Wine, and what is not an intoxicant is not wine by consensus, so it is established that wine is the intoxicant... And similarly in backbiting. There must therefore be a proof in the definition, whether it is a definition of a name or a definition according to reality. Not as the Greek logicians hold, that the definition conveys the conception of the quiddity in the mind of the listener and that this occurs merely by the statement of the definer without any proof at all. And their division of necessary attributes of the described into three kinds: essential attributes entering into the quiddity, external attributes necessary to the quiddity without its existence, and external attributes necessary to its existence. All of this is false as was shown before, if by quiddity they mean external existents. But if they mean by quiddity what is conceived in the mind, then the measure of that quiddity and its attribute follows the conception of the one who conceives. So if he wants to depict the quiddity of 'human' in the mind, the depiction differs according to the one who conceives: sometimes he conceives the quiddity of human as a sentient, growing, moving-by-will, speaking body; sometimes he conceives it as a rational animal; and sometimes he conceives it as a laughing animal or just laughing. All of this may be correct in itself, but it has no scientific benefit, and it never reaches objective truth; it merely returns to the conception of the speaker's intention, whether it is true or false. Therefore the Islamic scholars paid no attention to the logical rules of definition; in their view they are a mechanical device that achieves nothing. Indeed, definition for them is by a single necessary attribute, and a single necessary attribute suffices, so they do not mention genera, differentiae, or accidental universals, and they do not use them. (1) Ibn Taymiyya: al-Radd... p. 361.
Source pages p. 264

Commentary
This passage presents Ibn Taymiyya's critique of the Greek theory of definition (hadd). The author begins by illustrating a definitional argument: 'Wine is intoxicating' is established by consensus, showing that definition requires proof. Ibn Taymiyya then states categorically that every definition—whether nominal or real—must be supported by evidence, contrary to the Greek logicians who claim that a definition directly conveys the quiddity (mahiyya) to the listener's mind without any proof. Next, he attacks their division of necessary attributes into three categories: (1) essential attributes that are part of the quiddity, (2) external attributes necessary to the quiddity but not to its existence, and (3) external attributes necessary to its existence. He declares this classification false when applied to external existents. However, if by 'quiddity' they mean a mental concept, then the depiction varies according to the imaginer. For example, one may conceive 'human' as a rational animal or as a laughing animal; all these depictions may be correct subjectively but have no scientific value or objective truth. Consequently, Islamic scholars ignored these logical rules, considering them a sterile mechanism. Rather, they defined by a single necessary attribute (al-wasf al-wahid al-mulazim), without resorting to genera, differentiae, or accidental universals. This critique underscores Ibn Taymiyya's rejection of the epistemological pretensions of Aristotelian logic.

Key Terms

الحد
الأجناس
الفصول
العرض العام

Defining Qiyas al-Awla: The Superiority Argument
Author defines qiyas al-awla and its superiority feature.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the defining features of qiyas al-awla and distinguish it from simple analogy.

Core Idea
In qiyas al-awla, the conclusion is more deserving of truth than the premise.

Ibn Taymiyya revives a logical tool used by Ahmad ibn Hanbal and the Salaf, known as qiyas al-awla (argument from superiority). Unlike ordinary analogy where the conclusion is as true as the premise, here the conclusion is even more certain. This tool is especially powerful for reasoning about divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim
Look for a statement where something is attributed to God or another subject in a superior way. In the passage, Ibn Taymiyya says: 'Whatever perfection free of deficiency belongs to others, its affirmation for Him is by way of awla.'
The claim is that what is true of creatures in a limited way is even more true of God.
2
Extract the premise
Find the evidence used to support the claim. Here, the premise is that certain perfections exist in creatures without deficiency.
The premise is a known fact about creatures.
3
Notice the superiority relation
The conclusion is not just equal to the premise; it is 'awla' (more worthy). This is the core of qiyas al-awla.
The superiority is based on the greater perfection of God.
4
Compare with regular analogy
In regular analogy (tamthil), the conclusion holds to the same degree as the premise. In qiyas al-awla, the conclusion is stronger.
This makes qiyas al-awla a special case of analogical reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الطريق الثاني : قياس الأولى : يعرف ابن تيمية قياس الأولى بأنه ما يكون الحكم المطلوب فيه أولى بالثبوت من الصورة المذكورة في الدليل الدال عليه ، ويذكر أن أحمد بن حنبل وغيره من السلف ـ كانوا يستخدمونه . ويحاول ابن تيمية استخدام القياس في إحدى المشاكل الكلامية وهي مشكلة الصفات فيرى أن « ما لغير الله من كمال لا نقص فيه فثبوته له بطريق الأولى وما تنزه غيره فتنزيهه عنه بطريق الأولى » ويصور ابن تيمية الشطر الأولى من هذا التعريف في صورة عكسية . فيقول إن قياس الأولى « يدل على أنه ثبت له من صفات الكمال التي لا نقص فيها أكمل مما علموه ثابتاً لغيره مع التفاوت الذي لا يضبطه العقل » فما يثبت لله أعظم مما يثبت لكل ما سواه بما لا يحصر قدره ، ولهذا كان ينبغي إختيار الأسماء التي تطلق عليه وعلى غيره بطريق التشكيك الذي هو نوع من التواطىء العام ، لا بطريق الإشتراك اللفظي ولا بطريق الإشتراك المعنوي الذي تتماثل أفراده ، بل بطريق الإشتراك المعنوي الذي تتفاضل أفراده . ويشبه ابن تيمية هذا بلفظ البياض ، يطلق على الشديد كبياض الثلج وعلى ما هو أخف بياضاً من الثلج كالعاج مثلاً ويشبه هذا أيضاً بلفظ الوجود . إن الوجود ينقسم إلى واجب وممكن ، ولكن كون وجود الواجب أكمل من وجود الممكن لا يمنع مطلقاً من اتفاق الإثنين في مسمى الوجود ، ـ فالوجود معنى كلي مشترك ـ وهذا الأمر يحدث أيضاً في سائر أسمائه وصفاته التي تطلق على الخالق والمخلوقات . وقد أدى هذا إلى خلاف شديد بين فرق الإسلام . هل صفات الله وأسماؤه وغيرها حقيقة في الخالق ، مجاز في المخلوق ، أم المسألة بالعكس . أم أنها حقيقة في الإثنين ؟ يذكر ابن تيمية أن أغلب المسلمين أخذوا بالرأي الأخير ـ وهو الرأي الصائب ، لأن الأسماء المشككة هي متواطئة باعتبار القدر المشترك . ويرى إبن تيمية أنه لهذا السبب أدرج الفلاسفة المتقدمون المشككة تحت المتواطئة ، ولم يفردوا لها قسماً خاصاً . بل إعتبروها قسماً للمتواطئة العامة وقسماً للمتواطئة الخاصة . أما القسم الأول ، فلا بد فيه من إثبات قدر مشترك كلي وهو مسمى المتواطئة العامة، وهذا المسمى لا يكون إلا في الذهن ، وهذا هو مدلول القياس البرهاني » أما القسم الثاني : فلا بد فيه من إثبات التفاضل وهو مدلول المشككة التي هي قسم المتواطئة ، وذلك هو مدلول الأقيسة البرهانية القرآنية ، أي قياس
The second method: Qiyas al-Awla (argument from superiority). Ibn Taymiyya defines qiyas al-awla as that in which the required judgment is more worthy of affirmation than the form mentioned in the evidence indicating it. He mentions that Ahmad ibn Hanbal and other Salaf used it. Ibn Taymiyya attempts to use this syllogism in one of the theological problems, namely the problem of attributes. He opines: 'Whatever perfection free of deficiency belongs to others, its affirmation for Him is by way of awla (more worthy), and whatever others are free from, His transcendence thereof is by way of awla.' Ibn Taymiyya presents the first part of this definition inversely. He says that qiyas al-awla 'indicates that He possesses, of perfections without deficiency, more complete than what they know to be established for others, with a disparity that the intellect cannot grasp.' So what is established for God is greater than what is established for all else, to an extent that cannot be measured. Hence, one should choose names that apply to Him and to others by way of tashkik (equivocation with graded difference), which is a type of general tawatu' (univocity), not by way of purely verbal homonymy (ishtirak lafzi), nor by way of purely conceptual univocity (ishtirak ma'nawi) whose individuals are equal, but by way of conceptual univocity whose individuals are graded. Ibn Taymiyya compares this to the term 'whiteness', which applies to intense whiteness like snow and to lighter whiteness like ivory, for example; and similarly to the term 'existence'. Existence is divided into necessary and possible, but the fact that the existence of the Necessary is more perfect than the existence of the possible does not absolutely prevent the two from agreeing on the concept of existence—for existence is a common universal meaning. And this also occurs in all His other names and attributes that apply to the Creator and creatures. This led to intense disagreement among Islamic sects: Are God's attributes and names and others literally true of the Creator, metaphorical of creatures, or the opposite? Or are they literally true of both? Ibn Taymiyya mentions that most Muslims took the last view—and it is the correct view—because equivocal names (mushtarak mushakkik) are univocal (mutawati') in terms of the common element. Ibn Taymiyya holds that for this reason, the ancient philosophers included the equivocal under the univocal and did not make it a separate division. Rather, they considered it a division of general univocity and a division of specific univocity. As for the first division, it requires establishing a universal common element, which is the meaning of general univocity, and this meaning exists only in the mind, and this is the meaning of demonstrative syllogism. As for the second division: it requires establishing gradation, which is the meaning of the equivocal (mushakkika) that is a division of univocity, and that is the meaning of the Quranic demonstrative syllogisms, i.e., qiyas...
Source pages p. 278

Commentary
This passage introduces qiyas al-awla (argument from superiority) as defined by Ibn Taymiyya. It is a type of analogical reasoning where the conclusion is more deserving of truth than the premise. Ibn Taymiyya traces its use to Ahmad ibn Hanbal and the Salaf, and applies it to the theological problem of divine attributes. The key point is that any perfection in creation without deficiency is even more perfectly attributed to God (awla), and any imperfection from which others are free is even more fittingly negated of God. Ibn Taymiyya then inverts this: what we know of perfection in creatures is overshadowed by what is established for God, which is incomparably greater. This leads to the nature of divine names: they apply to God and creatures in a graded manner (tashkik), which is a form of general univocity (tawatu' 'amm), not pure homonymy or pure equality. He gives examples: 'whiteness' for snow and ivory, and 'existence' for God and creatures. Both share the same universal meaning but differ in intensity. This view resolves the theological dispute about whether divine names are literal or metaphorical. Ibn Taymiyya asserts that the majority of Muslims hold they are literally true of both (haqiqa fi'l-ithnayn). He then explains that the ancient philosophers subsumed equivocal (mushakkik) under univocal (mutawati'), making it a division thereof: one division is general univocity requiring a common universal in the mind, which is the domain of demonstrative syllogism; the other division is specific univocity involving gradation, corresponding to Quranic demonstrative syllogisms (like qiyas al-awla).

Key Terms
قياس الأولى
التشكيك
التواطىء العام

B) The conclusion is more worthy of affirmation than the premise
C) It applies only to negative attributes
D) It requires a universal premise

Key Takeaway
Qiyas al-awla is a superior analogical argument where the conclusion is more certain than the premise, essential for reasoning about divine perfections.
