Qiyas al-Awla and Divine Names: The Theory of Tashkik
Author applies qiyas al-awla to divine attributes and discusses tashkik.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how Ibn Taymiyya's qiyas al-awla supports the graded univocity (tashkik) of divine names.

Core Idea
Divine names share a meaning with creatures but differ in perfection.

Ibn Taymiyya uses qiyas al-awla to resolve the theological dispute about divine names: are they literal, metaphorical, or something in between? He argues that names like 'Living' or 'Powerful' apply to God and creatures in a graded manner (tashkik), just as 'whiteness' applies to snow and ivory. This avoids both pure equivocation (ishtirak lafzi) and pure univocity (tawatu' mutlaq).

Guided Path
1
State the problem of divine attributes
Different Islamic sects disagreed: Ash'aris said names are literal for God only, Mu'tazila said they are metaphorical, etc. The question is whether terms like 'alim (knowing) mean the same when applied to God and humans.
This is a central issue in Kalam.
2
Identify Ibn Taymiyya's qiyas al-awla for attributes
He applies the superiority argument: since knowledge in creatures is a perfection, it is even more true for God. But the meaning is not entirely different; it is the same basic meaning but with greater intensity.
This leads to the concept of tashkik.
3
Understand the example of 'whiteness'
Ibn Taymiyya says 'whiteness' applies to snow (intense) and to ivory (less intense). Both share the common meaning of whiteness, but differ in degree. Similarly, existence applies to God (necessary) and creatures (possible) with a common meaning but different grades.
Tashkik is univocity with gradation.
4
Recognize the solution: names are mushakkika
Ibn Taymiyya concludes that divine names are neither purely univocal (equal) nor purely equivocal (different meanings). They are mushakkika: univocal in the common meaning, but graded in perfection.
This preserves both God's transcendence and the meaningfulness of divine names.

Commentary
This passage introduces qiyas al-awla (argument from superiority) as defined by Ibn Taymiyya. It is a type of analogical reasoning where the conclusion is more deserving of truth than the premise. Ibn Taymiyya traces its use to Ahmad ibn Hanbal and the Salaf, and applies it to the theological problem of divine attributes. The key point is that any perfection in creation without deficiency is even more perfectly attributed to God (awla), and any imperfection from which others are free is even more fittingly negated of God. Ibn Taymiyya then inverts this: what we know of perfection in creatures is overshadowed by what is established for God, which is incomparably greater. This leads to the nature of divine names: they apply to God and creatures in a graded manner (tashkik), which is a form of general univocity (tawatu' 'amm), not pure homonymy or pure equality. He gives examples: 'whiteness' for snow and ivory, and 'existence' for God and creatures. Both share the same universal meaning but differ in intensity. This view resolves the theological dispute about whether divine names are literal or metaphorical. Ibn Taymiyya asserts that the majority of Muslims hold they are literally true of both (haqiqa fi'l-ithnayn). He then explains that the ancient philosophers subsumed equivocal (mushakkik) under univocal (mutawati'), making it a division thereof: one division is general univocity requiring a common universal in the mind, which is the domain of demonstrative syllogism; the other division is specific univocity involving gradation, corresponding to Quranic demonstrative syllogisms (like qiyas al-awla).

Key Terms
قياس الأولى
التشكيك
التواطىء العام
الإشتراك المعنوي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How does Ibn Taymiyya classify divine names according to the passage?
A) As purely literal for God and metaphorical for creatures
B) As purely equivocal (ishtirak lafzi)
C) As univocal with gradation (tashkik) in terms of a common meaning
D) As purely univocal with no difference in perfection

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya uses qiyas al-awla to establish that divine names are graded univocal (tashkik), resolving the tension between God's transcendence and the intelligibility of revelation.

Ibn Taymiyya's Classification of Univocal Terms
Author explains Ibn Taymiyya's classification of terms into mutawati' 'amm and khass, linking to divine names.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between mutawati' 'amm and mutawati' khass (mushakkik) according to Ibn Taymiyya and apply this classification to the theological debate on divine names.

Core Idea
Ibn Taymiyya subsumes graded univocal terms (mushakkik) under univocal terms (mutawati'), reshaping how divine names are understood.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya addresses a critical question in Islamic theology: When we say 'God exists' and 'man exists', do we mean the same thing? He uses the examples of whiteness and existence to show that these terms share a common meaning but differ in degree. He then classifies these 'mushakkik' terms as a subset of 'mutawati'' terms, dividing the latter into general (purely mental) and specific (graded). This classification resolves the debate on divine attributes between the camps of haqiqa, majaz, or both.

Guided Path
1
Identify the examples
Notice Ibn Taymiyya's examples of whiteness (applied to snow and ivory) and existence (applied to God and creatures). Both share a core meaning but differ in intensity. This is the hallmark of mushakkik terms.
The common meaning is preserved, but the degree differs.
2
Extract the classification
Find the distinction between mutawati' 'amma (general univocal) and mutawati' khassa (specific univocal). The general exists only in the mind and is used in demonstrative syllogisms. The specific involves differentiation (tafadul) and is the domain of mushakkik.
Ibn Taymiyya states that early philosophers did not treat mushakkik as a separate category but as a division of mutawati'.
3
See where mushakkik fits
Ibn Taymiyya explicitly says mushakkik is a division of mutawati' khassa. So mushakkik is not a separate category but a subtype of univocal terms. This is a key departure from later logicians.
Later logicians often list mushakkik as a third category alongside mutawati' and mushtarak.
4
Apply to divine names
Understand that this classification allows terms like 'life', 'knowledge', and 'existence' to be predicated of God and creatures with a common core meaning but an infinite difference in degree. This provides the basis for qiyas al-awla (argument from the greater) used in the Quran.
For example, 'For Allah is the truth, and whatever they invoke besides Him is falsehood' – if creation has some truth, the Creator has it more perfectly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويشبه ابن تيمية هذا بلفظ البياض ، يطلق على الشديد كبياض الثلج وعلى ما هو أخف بياضاً من الثلج كالعاج مثلاً ويشبه هذا أيضاً بلفظ الوجود . إن الوجود ينقسم إلى واجب وممكن ، ولكن كون وجود الواجب أكمل من وجود الممكن لا يمنع مطلقاً من اتفاق الإثنين في مسمى الوجود ، ـ فالوجود معنى كلي مشترك ـ وهذا الأمر يحدث أيضاً في سائر أسمائه وصفاته التي تطلق على الخالق والمخلوقات . وقد أدى هذا إلى خلاف شديد بين فرق الإسلام . هل صفات الله وأسماؤه وغيرها حقيقة في الخالق ، مجاز في المخلوق ، أم المسألة بالعكس . أم أنها حقيقة في الإثنين ؟ يذكر ابن تيمية أن أغلب المسلمين أخذوا بالرأي الأخير ـ وهو الرأي الصائب ، لأن الأسماء المشككة هي متواطئة باعتبار القدر المشترك . ويرى ابن تيمية أنه لهذا السبب أدرج الفلاسفة المتقدمون المشككة تحت المتواطئة ، ولم يفردوا لها قسماً خاصاً . بل إعتبروها قسماً للمتواطئة العامة وقسماً للمتواطئة الخاصة . أما القسم الأول ، فلا بد فيه من إثبات قدر مشترك كلي وهو مسمى المتواطئة العامة، وهذا المسمى لا يكون إلا في الذهن ، وهذا هو مدلول القياس البرهاني » أما القسم الثاني : فلا بد فيه من إثبات التفاضل وهو مدلول المشككة التي هي قسم المتواطئة ، وذلك هو مدلول الأقيسة البرهانية القرآنية ، أي قياس الأولى
Ibn Taymiyya compares this to the word 'whiteness', applied to intense white like snow and to lighter white like ivory. He also compares it to the word 'existence'. Existence is divided into necessary and possible, but the fact that the existence of the Necessary is more perfect than the existence of the possible does not absolutely prevent their agreement in the meaning of existence — for existence is a common universal meaning. This also occurs in all His names and attributes applied to the Creator and creatures. This led to intense disagreement among Islamic sects: Are God's attributes and names real (haqiqa) in the Creator, metaphorical (majaz) in the creature, or the opposite? Or are they real in both? Ibn Taymiyya mentions that most Muslims took the latter view — and it is the correct view, because mushakkik names are mutawati' (univocal) with respect to the common element. Ibn Taymiyya holds that for this reason the early philosophers included mushakkik under mutawati', and did not make it a separate category. Rather, they considered it a division of general mutawati' and a division of specific mutawati'. As for the first division, it requires establishing a common universal predicate — that is the meaning of general mutawati', and this meaning exists only in the mind, and this is the denotation of demonstrative syllogism. As for the second division, it requires establishing differentiation (tafadul) — that is the meaning of mushakkik, which is a division of mutawati', and that is the denotation of Quranic demonstrative syllogisms, namely qiyas al-awla.
Source pages p. 278

Commentary
This passage explains Ibn Taymiyya's view on the nature of terms applied to God and creatures. He uses the analogy of whiteness and existence to show that such terms are not purely equivocal (mushtarak) nor purely univocal (mutawati') in the sense of complete equality, but rather mushakkik (graded univocal). The early philosophers considered mushakkik as a subset of mutawati' because of the common core meaning. Ibn Taymiyya further divides mutawati' into general (al-'amma) and specific (al-khassa). The general is purely conceptual and the basis of demonstrative syllogism, while the specific involves degrees and is the basis of Quranic arguments like qiyas al-awla.

Key Terms
مشككة (mushakkika): Names that are univocal in meaning but differ in degree (e.g., whiteness, existence when applied to God and creatures). [Note: Ibn Taymiyya treats mushakkik as a subset of mutawati', contrary to some later logicians who made it a separate category.]
متواطئة (mutawāṭi'a): Univocal terms that share a common meaning; divided into general (purely conceptual) and specific (including degrees). [Note: Classical logicians often distinguished mutawati' from mushakkik; Ibn Taymiyya subsumes mushakkik under mutawati'.]
متواطئة عامة (mutawāṭi'a 'āmma): The common universal meaning existing only in the mind, used in demonstrative syllogisms.
متواطئة خاصة (mutawāṭi'a khāṣṣa): Terms that include differentiation (tafadul), i.e., mushakkik names, used in Quranic arguments like qiyas al-awla.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya, the term 'existence' when applied to God and man is an example of which category?
A) Mushtarak (equivocal)
B) Mutawati' 'amm (general univocal)
C) Mutawati' khass (specific univocal / mushakkik)
D) Haqiqa only (real in God only)

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya's classification of mushakkik as a subset of mutawati' provides a nuanced understanding of divine names that avoids both anthropomorphism and equivocation.

Qiyas al-Awla: The Only Valid Analogy for Allah
Ibn Qayyim's classification of analogies for divine attributes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish qiyas al-awla from qiyas al-tamthil and qiyas al-shuhud, and explain why only qiyas al-awla is valid for knowing Allah.

Core Idea
Only qiyas al-awla is valid for Allah — the rest compare Him to creation.

Ibn Qayyim al-Jawziyya, following his teacher Ibn Taymiyya, argued that the only valid form of analogical reasoning to know Allah is qiyas al-awla (priority analogy). This lesson unpacks the distinction between three types of qiyas and the theological rationale behind excluding those that require equality.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of qiyas
List the three forms of analogy mentioned by Ibn Qayyim: qiyas al-awla, qiyas al-tamthil, and qiyas al-shuhud. Note their definitions and how they differ.
Qiyas tamthil is analogy of equality; qiyas shuhud is analogy of observation; qiyas awla is priority analogy.
2
Understand why qiyas tamthil and shuhud are invalid
Explain why Ibn Qayyim rules out qiyas tamthil and shuhud: they assume equality or similarity between the compared individuals, but nothing equals Allah or can be observed alongside Him.
These analogies are 'impossible to establish regarding Him' because they would imply limitation or similarity.
3
Grasp the logic of qiyas al-awla
Understand that qiyas al-awla infers a higher attribute from a lower one: if a creature possesses a perfection, the Creator possesses it more perfectly. It does not rely on equality but on priority.
This form is used in rational and revealed proofs for Allah's attributes.
4
Assess Ibn Qayyim's originality
Note the author's comment that Ibn Qayyim adds nothing new to Ibn Taymiyya's views. Consider how this reflects the transmission of ideas within a scholarly tradition.
The passage highlights that Ibn Qayyim did not write a separate critique of Aristotelian logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
معرفة الله . وأن الطريق الوحيد لمعرفة الله ووجوده هو قياس الأولى « الرب تعالى لا يدخل مع خلقه في قياس تمثيل ولا قياس شهود يستوي أفراده ، فهذان الفرعان من القياس يستحيل ثبوتها في حقه . وأما قياس الأولى فهو غير مستحيل في حقه . بل هو واجب له وهو مستعمل في حقه عقلاً ونقلاً(۱) » ثم يشرح هذا القياس بما لا يخرج عن كتابات ابن تيمية . ليس في كلام ابن القيم شيء جديد ؛ بل هو يعبر عن بعض آراء ابن تيمية جملة ، ويكاد يورد بعض عباراته بنصها . ولم يفرد ابن القيم لنقد المنطق الأرسططاليسي كتاباً خاصاً . ولم يتجه في هذا النقد نحو فكرة أو أفكار معينة بالذات .
Knowledge of Allah. And that the only way to know Allah and His existence is qiyas al-awla (priority analogy): 'The Lord, Most High, does not enter with His creation into a qiyas tamthil (analogy of equality) nor a qiyas shuhud (witnessing analogy) whose individuals are equal. These two branches of qiyas are impossible to establish regarding Him. As for qiyas al-awla, it is not impossible regarding Him. Rather, it is obligatory for Him and it is used regarding Him by reason and revelation.' Then he explains this qiyas in a way that does not go beyond the writings of Ibn Taymiyya. There is nothing new in Ibn Qayyim's words; rather, he expresses some of Ibn Taymiyya's opinions as a whole, and nearly quotes some of his phrases verbatim. Ibn Qayyim did not devote a separate book to the critique of Aristotelian logic. And he did not direct this critique towards a specific idea or ideas in particular.
Source pages p. 283

Commentary
This passage discusses the view of Ibn Qayyim regarding the type of qiyas (analogy) that can be used to know Allah. The author first quotes Ibn Qayyim's statement that the only path to knowledge of Allah and His existence is qiyas al-awla (priority analogy). Ibn Qayyim explains that Allah cannot be subjected to qiyas al-tamthil (analogy of equality) or qiyas al-shuhud (analogy of observation) because these involve comparing equals, and nothing is equal to Allah. However, qiyas al-awla, which infers a higher attribute from a lower one, is not only possible but obligatory regarding Allah, and is used both rationally and textually. Then the author notes that Ibn Qayyim's explanation of this qiyas does not go beyond the writings of Ibn Taymiyya, meaning he merely repeats his teacher's ideas. In fact, the author states that there is nothing new in Ibn Qayyim's words; he expresses Ibn Taymiyya's views and sometimes quotes his exact phrases. The passage also notes that Ibn Qayyim did not write a separate book dedicated to critiquing Aristotelian logic, nor did he focus his critique on any specific doctrine. This indicates that Ibn Qayyim's critique was general and not specialized, unlike later critics.

Key Terms
قياس الأولى
قياس تمثيل
قياس شهود

B) Qiyas al-shuhud
C) Qiyas al-awla

Key Takeaway
Qiyas al-awla is the only analogical method permissible for knowing Allah, because it relies on priority rather than equality.

Al-San'ani's Critique of Greek Logic and Defense of Quranic Reasoning
Author describes al-San'ani's book arguing that Quranic reasoning methods are superior to Greek logic and sufficient for knowledge of God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct al-San'ani's argument for the superiority of Quranic reasoning over Greek logic and evaluate the grounds he provides.

Core Idea
Al-San'ani argues that the Quran's rational proofs surpass Greek logic, being clearer, accessible, and free from artificiality.

This lesson explores the critique of Greek logic by the Shiite scholar al-Wazir al-San'ani (d. 840 AH). Influenced by Ibn Taymiyya, al-San'ani wrote 'Tarjih Asalib al-Qur'an 'ala Asalib al-Yunan' to argue that the Quran's methods of inference are superior to those of the Greeks. As you read the passage, you will learn how al-San'ani grounds his claim in the Quran's comprehensiveness, its freedom from artificiality, and its accessibility to all people. This critique is part of a broader Islamic intellectual tradition that questions the necessity of Greek logic for knowing God.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target of critique
Al-San'ani attacks the idea that Greek logic is necessary for theological knowledge. He points to those who 'urge returning to the laws of the innovators and the Greeks' and belittle reliance on the Quran.
Notice he does not reject all logic, but specifically the sufficiency of Greek methods.
2
Extract al-San'ani's main claims about the Quran
He claims the Quran contains 'the most correct sciences', 'the clearest in minds', 'rational proofs that transcend logic and kalam', and is 'free from artificiality'.
These are positive assertions about the Quran's intrinsic logical superiority.
3
Analyze the reasons he gives
He argues that the Quran is comprehensive for elite and commoners, avoids affectation, and includes proofs for tawhid. He also cites consensus of Muslims and traditions from Ahl al-Bayt.
His reasoning combines scriptural authority, consensus, and pragmatic accessibility.
4
Assess the strength of his argument
Al-San'ani does not provide a systematic comparison but relies on general claims and selected examples. His internal validity hinges on whether the Quran indeed contains all necessary rational proofs without interpretation.
Consider whether his critique is convincing and what assumptions he makes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهذا الإتجاه المعين في نقد المنطق الأرسططاليسي نحو فكرة بذاتها نجده لدى مفكر شيعي هو الوزير الصنعاني ( ٨٤٠هـ ) فقد كتب الصنعاني تحت تأثير ابن تيمية - إلى حد ما - كتابه « ترجيح أساليب القرآن على أساليب اليونان » . والفكرة التي يحاول الصنعاني أن يوضحها في كتابه : هي أن للقرآن أساليب في الإستدلال تخالف أساليب اليونان . وسيتبين هذا مما سنعرضه من كلامه . يرى الصنعاني أن القرآن قد جمع بين دفتيه أصح العلوم وأوضحها في العقول ، كما ضم أفضل الأعمال وأيسرها على الناس واشتمل من البراهين العقلية ما يسمو على فني المنطق والكلام ، لأن القرآن كتاب شامل عام للخواص والعوام ، وقد سلم مما اشتمل عليه المنطق والكلام من تصنع وتكلف في البديهيات والمسلمات ، وليس فيه أبداً أساليب الفلاسفة والمتكلمين الصناعية . ثم يذكر « أنه نبغ في هذا الزمان من عادى علوم القرآن وفارق فريق الفرقان وصنف في التحذير من الإعتماد على ما فيه من التباين في معرفة الديان وأصول قواعد الأديان ، وحث على الرجوع في ذلك الى معرفة قوانين المبتدعة واليونان ، منتقصاً لمن اكتفى بما في معجز التنزيل من البرهان . مقيماً لتلقي كثير من محكماته بالقبول والإيمان . ثم يعلل الداعي إلى تأليفه كتابه فيقول هو « نقض ما أدعاه وفي بيان بعض ما اشتمل عليه كتاب الله من الدلائل الغنية في الإعتقاد عن الإشغال بكتب الأوائل . ويفرد الصنعاني بعد ذلك فصلاً طويلاً في أن من يقول بقصور القرآن عن الوفاء بالدلالة على الربوبية والتوحيد والنبوات باطل ومخالف للمعقول والمنقول وإجماع المسلمين . ويحلل في هذا الفصل بعض الآيات القرآنية ويذكر أنها دلائل على أن القرآن هو الطريق الوحيد لإثبات وجود الله ويذكر إجماع المسلمين من جميع الطوائف على أن القرآن يفيد معرفة أدلة التوحيد من غير ظن ولا تقليد . وكما أن المتكلم ينظر في كتب شيوخه ليتعلم منها الأدلة من غير تقليد غيره ، فكذلك من نظر في القرآن ، يتعلم منه الأدلة من غير تقليد . بل القرآن الذي منه تعلم المتكلمون النظر ، لكنهم غلوا فيه . ولم يقتصروا على القدر الكافي النافع المذكور في كتاب الله . ويورد الصنعاني ما تيسر « من نصوص أهل البيت عليهم السلام على الإكتفاء بالجمل والحث على ذلك وكراهة الغلو في علم الكلام . ويقرر أن التدقيق الفلسفي في التوحيد ينتج الفساد ، لأن الإسلام دين الفطرة من غير احتياج إلى فلسفة .
And this particular trend in critiquing Aristotelian logic towards a specific idea is found with a Shiite thinker, al-Wazir al-San'ani (d. 840 AH). Al-San'ani wrote, under the influence of Ibn Taymiyya to some extent, his book 'Tarjih Asalib al-Qur'an 'ala Asalib al-Yunan' (Preferring the Methods of the Quran over the Methods of the Greeks). The idea that al-San'ani tries to clarify in his book is that the Quran has methods of inference that differ from the methods of the Greeks. This will become clear from what we present of his words. Al-San'ani holds that the Quran contains between its covers the most correct sciences and the clearest in minds, as it includes the best deeds and the easiest for people, and it comprises rational proofs that transcend the two arts of logic and kalam, because the Quran is a comprehensive book general for the elite and the commoners, and it is free from the artificiality and affectation that logic and kalam contain concerning self-evident and accepted premises. It never contains the artificial methods of the philosophers and theologians. Then he mentions: 'There arose in this time one who opposed the sciences of the Quran and left the party of the Furqan (criterion), and wrote warning against relying on what it contains of disparity in knowing the Lord and the foundational principles of religions, and urged returning in that to knowing the laws of the innovators and the Greeks, belittling the one who suffices with what is in the miraculous revelation of proof. He considered it acceptable to accept many of its definitive verses with submission and faith.' Then he explains the motive for authoring his book, saying: 'It is a refutation of what he claimed and an exposition of some of what the Book of Allah contains of rich evidences in belief, dispensing with the preoccupation with the books of the ancients.' After that, al-San'ani devotes a long chapter to the idea that whoever claims the Quran falls short of providing evidence for divinity, oneness, and prophethood is false and opposes reason, tradition, and the consensus of Muslims. In this chapter, he analyzes some Quranic verses and mentions that they are evidences that the Quran is the only path to prove the existence of Allah, and he mentions the consensus of Muslims from all sects that the Quran provides knowledge of the proofs of tawhid without conjecture or blind imitation. Just as the mutakallim (theologian) looks at the books of his teachers to learn proofs from them without imitating others, likewise whoever looks at the Quran learns proofs from it without blind imitation. Rather, the Quran is from which the mutakallimun learned to reason, but they exaggerated in it. They did not restrict themselves to the sufficient and beneficial extent mentioned in the Book of Allah. Al-San'ani also presents what he could of 'texts from the Ahl al-Bayt (peace be upon them) on sufficing with the concise and urging that and disliking exaggeration in the science of kalam.' And he affirms that philosophical precision in tawhid produces corruption, because Islam is the religion of innate human nature (fitra) without need for philosophy.
Source pages p. 283

Commentary
This passage introduces the Shiite scholar al-Wazir al-San'ani and his book 'Tarjih Asalib al-Qur'an 'ala Asalib al-Yunan'. The author states that al-San'ani, influenced by Ibn Taymiyya, wrote this work to argue that the Quran's methods of inference are superior to and different from Greek logic. Al-San'ani believes the Quran contains the most correct and clearest sciences and includes rational proofs that surpass logic and theology (kalam). He criticizes those who downgrade the Quran's sufficiency for proof and urge reliance on Greek-derived logic. Al-San'ani's motive for his book is to refute such claims and show that the Quran provides ample evidence for belief in Allah's existence and tawhid. He dedicates a chapter to arguing that the Quran alone suffices for establishing theological truths, and that this is supported by consensus of Muslims. He also draws an analogy: just as a theologian learns from his teachers without blind imitation, one can learn from the Quran directly without imitation. The theologians themselves originally learned reasoning from the Quran, but they later exaggerated and went beyond what is sufficient. Al-San'ani also cites traditions from the Ahl al-Bayt emphasizing sufficiency with the Quran and dislike for excessive kalam. Finally, he declares that philosophical detail in tawhid leads to corruption, since Islam is based on fitra (natural disposition) without needing philosophy.

ترجيح أساليب القرآن على أساليب اليونان
أساليب الإستدلال
الفطرة

Al-San'ani's critique asserts that the Quran's inherent rational proofs are sufficient for knowledge of God, eliminating the need for Greek logic.

Al-Subki's Conditional Permissibility of Logic
Al-Subki defines conditions for permissible logic study.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the prerequisites and conditions for studying logic according to al-Subki and apply them to evaluate whether a given scholar may study logic.

Core Idea
Logic's permissibility depends on the user's religious grounding and method.

In this passage, the Shafi'i jurist 'Abd al-Wahhab al-Subki navigates the controversy over studying logic. He avoids both blanket prohibition and blanket permission, instead laying out strict criteria. Understanding his position clarifies how Islamic scholars balanced intellectual tools with spiritual safety.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories
Al-Subki distinguishes between two groups: those for whom logic is prohibited and those for whom it is permissible. Start by locating these in the text.
Prohibited: those lacking firm religious foundation. Permissible: mature scholars meeting conditions.
2
List the prerequisites for permissible study
Before even considering the conditions, al-Subki requires the scholar to have deep religious knowledge: firm Sharia, memorization of Quran and hadith, and status as a recognized jurist/mufti. Extract these from the passage.
These prerequisites ensure the scholar is not vulnerable to philosophical confusion.
3
Extract the two conditions
Al-Subki adds two conditions for those who are permitted: (1) unshakable faith, (2) no mixing of philosophical language with Islamic discourse. Find these in the Arabic.
The second condition is crucial: mixing (mazj) causes harm.
4
Understand the exceptional cases
Al-Subki acknowledges that al-Ghazali and al-Razi mixed philosophy with kalam but excuses them due to their supreme religious station. Identify why they are exceptions.
Their deep knowledge made them immune to harm; they serve as models for those who reach their level.
5
Apply the criteria to a hypothetical case
Consider a scholar who has memorized the Quran and important hadith collections, is a recognized mufti, but is new to logic. Would al-Subki permit him? Use the conditions to decide.
Focus on the confidence condition: the scholar must be certain his faith won't be shaken.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما القسم الثاني فهو الذي بحث في مسألة التحريم ، فأهم ممثليه فقيه شافعي ممتاز هو عبد الوهاب السبكي ( ٧٧١هـ ) . ولم يوافق السبكي على تحريم المنطق إطلاقاً ، بل أتى برأي بين التحليل والتحريم فيحرمه هو والفلسفة على من لم ترسخ قواعد الشريعة في قلبه ، ويمتلىء جوفه من عظمة هذا النبي الكريم وشريعته ، ويحفظ القرآن العزيز وشيئاً كثيراً جداً من حديث النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم على طريقة المحدثين ، ويعرف من فروع الفقه ما فيه يسمى فقيهاً مفتياً مشاراً إليه من أهل مذهبه إذا وقعت حادثة فقهية ، وأما من وصل إلى هذا المقام الديني وتسلح بكل هذا فله النظر فيها للرد على المناطقة والمتعلقة ولكن بشرطين : أحدهما ، أن يثق من نفسه بأنه وصل إلى درجة لا تزعزعها رياح الأباطيل وشبه الأضاليل وأهواء الملاحدة . والثاني ، ان لا يمزج كلامهم بكلام علماء الإسلام . ويعترف السبكي بأنه قد حصل ضرر عظيم على المسلمين بمزج كلام الفلسفة بكلام المتكلمين . وأدى الحال إلى طعن المشبهة والحشوية والمجسمة من رعاع الخلق في الأشاعرة والشوافع . وهو يقصد ابن تيمية . ويهاجم نصير الدين الطوسي وغيره من أتباعه ممن قاموا بالمزج أما أن حجة الإسلام الغزالي والإمام فخر الدين الرازي قد خاضوا في علوم الفلسفة ودونوها وخلطوها بكلام المتكلمين فهو يعترف به . ولكنه لا ينكر إن هذين إمامان جليلان عظيمان ، ولم يخض واحد منهما في هذه العلوم ، حتى صار مكتملاً في الدين وأصبحا مثلاً كبرى في معرفة علم الكلام على طريقة أهل السنة والجماعة . فمن وصل إلى مقامهما ، لا يلام عليه النظر في الكتب الفلسفية ولا يمنع ، بل هو مثاب مأجور
As for the second section, it is what investigated the issue of prohibition. Its most important representative is an excellent Shafi'i jurist, 'Abd al-Wahhab al-Subki (771 AH). Al-Subki did not agree with the absolute prohibition of logic; rather, he came with a view between permissibility and prohibition. He prohibits it and philosophy for one whose foundations of Sharia are not firmly established in his heart, who is filled with the greatness of this noble Prophet and his Sharia, who preserves the noble Quran and a great deal of the hadith of the Prophet (peace be upon him) in the manner of the hadith scholars, and who knows from the branches of jurisprudence enough to be called a jurist, a mufti, pointed to among the people of his school when a legal incident occurs. As for one who has reached this religious station and is armed with all of this, he may examine them to refute the logicians and related matters, but with two conditions: First, that he is confident of himself that he has reached a degree that cannot be shaken by the winds of falsehoods, the doubts of misguidance, and the desires of atheists. Second, that he does not mix their speech with the speech of Islamic scholars. Al-Subki acknowledges that great harm has befallen Muslims by mixing the speech of philosophy with the speech of the theologians (mutakallimun). This led to the attacks of the anthropomorphists, the hashwiyya, and the corporealists from the rabble against the Ash'aris and Shafi'is. He means Ibn Taymiyya. He attacks Nasir al-Din al-Tusi and others of his followers who engaged in mixing. As for Hujjat al-Islam al-Ghazali and Imam Fakhr al-Din al-Razi, they waded into the sciences of philosophy and recorded them and mixed them with the speech of the theologians—this he acknowledges. But he does not deny that these two are great, magnificent imams, and neither of them waded into these sciences until he became complete in religion and became supreme examples in knowledge of kalam according to the way of Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jama'a. Whoever reaches their station is not blamed for looking into philosophical books nor prevented; rather, he is rewarded and recompensed.
Source pages p. 289

Commentary
This passage presents the position of the Shafi'i jurist 'Abd al-Wahhab al-Subki on the permissibility of studying logic and philosophy. Al-Subki takes a middle ground between absolute prohibition and absolute permissibility. He argues that logic and philosophy are forbidden for those who are not deeply grounded in Islamic law—specifically, those who have not firmly internalized the principles of Sharia, have not memorized the Qur'an and a significant body of hadith with proper methodology, and are not recognized jurists. For such people, engaging with logic is harmful. However, for a scholar who has reached a high level of religious knowledge and spiritual maturity, it is permissible but with two strict conditions: (1) he must be certain that his faith is unshakable by doubts or atheistic ideas, and (2) he must never mix the language of the logicians/philosophers with the language of Islamic scholars. Al-Subki warns that mixing philosophical discourse with kalam (theology) has caused great harm, leading to accusations against Ash'aris and Shafi'is by simplistic anthropomorphists. He specifically criticizes Nasir al-Din al-Tusi and his followers for this mixing. However, he makes an exception for al-Ghazali and Fakhr al-Din al-Razi, who were so complete in their religious knowledge that their engagement with philosophy was safe and even commendable. The passage thus outlines a nuanced ruling that balances the dangers of philosophical contamination with the necessity of refutation.

السبكي
المزج

Suhrawardi's Critique of Aristotelian Definition
Suhrawardi critiques the Aristotelian definition by genus and specific difference, proposing alternative paths to knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Suhrawardi's arguments against the Aristotelian definition by genus and specific difference, and identify his proposed alternatives.

Core Idea
The Aristotelian definition collapses because its essential components are unknowable.

Suhrawardi launches a devastating critique of the Aristotelian definition (hadd). He points out a fatal contradiction: the specific difference (fasl) is non-sensible and unknown, yet it must serve as a known element in definition. Moreover, he argues that no definer can be certain of enumerating all essential attributes (dhatiyyat) of a thing. These epistemological objections lead him to reject the composite definition entirely and propose alternative paths: sensory experience or mystical unveiling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Aristotelian definition formula
According to the passage, the Aristotelians define by combining genus (jins) and specific difference (fasl). Both are considered essential (dhati).
2
Find the contradiction Suhrawardi exposes
The rule: the unknown is reached through the known. But the specific difference is itself unknown (non-sensible), so it cannot be a known means.
3
Examine Suhrawardi's attack on enumerating essential attributes
Suhrawardi argues that a definer can never be sure all essential attributes have been captured; hidden ones may exist.
He states: 'Whoever mentions what he knows of the essential properties – he is not safe from the existence of another essential property he was heedless of.'
4
Understand the implication for certainty
If an essential attribute could be missing, knowledge of the quiddity is not certain but doubtful. Therefore, hadd is impossible.
5
Identify Suhrawardi's alternative paths
Sensory perception for sensible things, and mystical unveiling (kashf wa 'iyān) – the latter being more precise.

وينقسم هذا المنطق الإشراقي عند السهروردي إلى : مبحث التعريف الإشراقي ومبحث القضايا الإشراقية . ومبحث القياس الإشراقي . أما مبحث التعريف . فللسهروردي نحوه موقف مزدوج . فهو أولاً ينكر التعريف الأرسططاليسي ، ثم يضع ثانياً مبحثه الخاص عن التعريف . يبدأ السهروردي نقده للتعريف الأرسططاليسي بعرض آراء الأرسططاليسيين والمتكلمين في التعريف ، واختلافهما فيه ، ونقض الأخيرين لاستناد التعريف بالحد إلى فكرة الماهية أي الجنس والفصل (٢) . ثم يضع نقده هو للتعريف الأرسططاليسي . فيذكر أن الأرسططاليسيين يذهبون إلى أن التعريف يتكون من الذاتيين أي الجنس والفصل هذا من ناحية ، ومن ناحية أخرى يقررون أن المجهول لا يتوصل إليه إلا بالمعلوم ، وهذا يؤدي إلى إنهيار مبحث التعريف بالحد جميعه ، لأن الفصل ليس إلا الصفات الكلية التي يتميز بها أفراد حقيقة واحدة عن أفراد غيرها من الحقائق التي تشترك معها في جنس واحد . فالفصل إذن هو الذاتي الخاص ، فإذا وجد في غير المحدود ، لم يكن خاصاً ، وقد اعتبر خاصاً به وغير محسوس ، فهو مجهول مع الشيء . فلا يمكن التعريف به ، لوجوب تقدم العلم بالمعرف على العلم بالمعرف . أما إذا عرف بالأمور العامة - أي الشاملة له ولغيره فلا تختص به هو بالذات - فلا يكون تعريفاً خاصاً ، كما افترض من قبل . والطريق الحقيقي للتعريف هو : إما طريق الاحساس فالأمور المحسوسة تدرك تمام الادراك . وإما طريق الكشف والعيان ـ وهو أدق الطرق وأوثقها(۱) . ثم يهاجم السهروردي فكرة الذاتيات وقيام التعريف عليها . فيقول « من ذكر ما عرف من الذاتيات ـ لم يأمن وجود ذاتي آخر غفل عنه . وللمستشرح أو المنازع أن يطالبه بذلك . وليس للمعرف حينئذ أن يقول لو كانت صفة أخرى لاطلعت عليها إذ كثيرة الصفات غير ظاهرة » يقصد السهروردي بذلك أن الطاقة الإنسانية لا تستطيع الإحاطة بجميع ذاتيات الحقيقة التي يطلب حدها . فمعارف الإنسان ضئيلة تماماً مع عدم تمكنه الوصول إلى كنه كثير من الأشياء . أما التعلل بأنه لو كان للحقيقة ذاتي آخر ، ما استطعنا الوصول إلى الماهية بدونه ، وعلى هذا الأساس ينكرون أي ذاتي آخر للماهية ، فتعليل خاطىء « لأن الحقيقة إنما تكون عرفت ، إذا عرف جميع ذاتياتها . فإذا انقدح جواز ذاتي ، لم تكن معرفة الحقيقة متيقنة ، بل تكون مشكوكة . ويستخلص السهروردي من هذا أن الإتيان بالحد كما إلتزم المشاؤون ـ أي تركيبيه من الجنس والفصل ـ غير ممكن . ويعلل الشارح الشيرازي هذا إما بجواز الاخلال بذاتي لم يعرف ، وإما لصعوبة تمييز الأجناس والفصول من اللوازم العامة والخاصة . ولهذا عدل المشاؤون إلى الرسوم المؤلفة من الخواص . ويقرر السهروردي آخر الأمر أن « صاحب المشائين » أي أرسطوطاليس ـ اعترف بصعوبة الإتيان بالحد(۲) . هذا هو موقف السهروردي تجاه التعريف الحقيقي الأرسططاليسي . ولكنه لم يكتف بهذا الموقف الهادم . بل وضع مبحثاً للتعريف ، نستطيع أن نعرضه طبقاً للشذرات القليلة التي تركها لنا في كتابه حكمة الإشراق وبعض الجمل التي نقلها القطب الشيرازي عن كتاب المطارحات للسهروردي .
And this Illuminationist logic according to Suhrawardi is divided into: the topic of Illuminationist definition, the topic of Illuminationist propositions, and the topic of Illuminationist syllogism. As for the topic of definition, Suhrawardi has a dual stance towards it. First, he denies the Aristotelian definition, then secondly he lays down his own topic on definition. Suhrawardi begins his critique of the Aristotelian definition by presenting the views of the Aristotelians and the theologians on definition, their disagreement therein, and the refutation by the latter of the reliance of definition by limit (hadd) on the idea of quiddity, i.e., genus and specific difference. Then he presents his own critique of the Aristotelian definition. He mentions that the Aristotelians hold that the definition consists of the two essential properties, i.e., genus and specific difference. On the one hand, and on the other hand, they affirm that the unknown cannot be reached except through the known, and this leads to the collapse of the whole topic of definition by limit, because the specific difference is nothing but the universal attributes by which individuals of one reality are distinguished from individuals of other realities that share with them in one genus. Thus the specific difference is the particular essential property. So if it is found in something other than the defined thing, it is not particular, and it is considered particular to it and non-sensible, so it is unknown along with the thing. Therefore, it is impossible to define by it, because knowledge of the thing known must precede knowledge of the thing known. However, if it is defined by general matters – i.e., those common to it and to others, not specific to it essentially – then it is not a particular definition, as previously assumed. The true path to definition is either the path of sensation, for sensible things are fully perceived, or the path of unveiling and ocular vision – which is the most precise and reliable path. Then Suhrawardi attacks the idea of essential properties and the foundation of definition on them. He says: 'Whoever mentions what he knows of the essential properties – he is not safe from the existence of another essential property he was heedless of. The person seeking explanation or the disputant can demand that of him. And the definer then cannot say: if there were another attribute, I would have been aware of it, since many attributes are not apparent.' By that, Suhrawardi means that human capacity cannot encompass all the essential properties of the reality whose limit is sought. Human knowledge is quite meager, along with his inability to reach the core of many things. As for the excuse that if the reality had another essential property, we would not be able to reach the quiddity without it, and on this basis they deny any other essential property of the quiddity, it is a mistaken reasoning 'because the reality is only known if all its essential properties are known. So if the possibility of an essential property occurs [to the mind], then knowledge of the reality is not certain, but rather doubtful.' Suhrawardi concludes from this that bringing the limit (hadd) as the Peripatetics committed to – i.e., its composition from genus and specific difference – is impossible. The commentator al-Shirazi explains this either by the possibility of missing an essential property that was not known, or by the difficulty of distinguishing genera and specific differences from general and specific necessary concomitants. That is why the Peripatetics turned to descriptions (rusum) composed of properties. And Suhrawardi finally affirms that 'the Master of the Peripatetics', i.e., Aristotle, admitted the difficulty of bringing the limit. This is Suhrawardi's stance towards the true Aristotelian definition. But he was not content with this demolishing stance. Rather, he laid down a topic on definition, which we can present according to the few fragments he left us in his book Hikmat al-Ishraq and some sentences transmitted by al-Qutb al-Shirazi from Suhrawardi's book al-Mutarahat.
Source pages p. 302

Commentary
This passage is a critical exposition of Suhrawardi's rejection of the Aristotelian definition by genus and specific difference (hadd). The author of Manahij Bahth explains Suhrawardi's dual stance: first denying the Aristotelian definition, then presenting his own. Suhrawardi begins by noting the disagreement between Aristotelians and theologians. The Aristotelians hold that definition consists of two essential properties (dhatiyyat): genus (jins) and specific difference (fasl). But Suhrawardi points out a contradiction: they also maintain that the unknown is only reached through the known. Since the specific difference is itself unknown (being non-sensible and particular to the defined thing), it cannot serve as a known means to define something else. If instead one uses general attributes, the definition loses specificity. Suhrawardi then presents the only valid paths to definition: sensory perception or mystical unveiling (kashf wa 'iyān), the latter being more precise. He further attacks the concept of essential properties, arguing that a definer can never be certain that he has enumerated all essential attributes of a thing, as some may be hidden. The claim that if there were another essential attribute we would know the thing through it is flawed, because true knowledge requires knowing all essential attributes; if any could be missing, certainty is lost. Therefore, the composite definition by genus and species is impossible. The commentator al-Shirazi notes that the Peripatetics themselves recognized this difficulty and resorted to descriptions (rusum) composed of accidental properties rather than essential ones. Suhrawardi even claims that Aristotle admitted the difficulty. After this demolition, Suhrawardi proposes his own theory of definition, based on fragments from his works.

Key Terms
حد
رسم
جنس
فصل
ذاتي

D) Because Suhrawardi believes only sensory definition is valid.

Key Takeaway
Suhrawardi's critique shows that definition by genus and species is epistemologically flawed, shifting the focus to alternative methods like sensory experience and mystical unveiling.

Suhrawardi's Epistemological Critique of Hadd
Suhrawardi argues that human knowledge cannot encompass all essential properties, making hadd impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Suhrawardi's argument against the possibility of certain knowledge via hadd and evaluate its force.

Core Idea
Human certainty about essences is impossible; therefore, hadd as perfect definition is unattainable.

This lesson examines Suhrawardi's radical critique of the Aristotelian theory of definition. While earlier logicians believed that a complete definition (hadd) capturing the essence was the ideal, Suhrawardi argues that human cognition cannot grasp all essential properties. This forces us to reconsider what definitions can achieve and leads to a turn toward descriptive definitions (rusum).

Guided Path
1
Identify the target of critique
What exactly is Suhrawardi attacking? He is targeting the Aristotelian hadd, which is a definition composed of genus (jins) and differentia (fasl) that claims to fully capture the essence (mahiyya) of a thing.
Hadd is considered the gold standard of definition in Peripatetic logic.
2
Extract the epistemological premise
Suhrawardi starts from a fundamental claim: human beings cannot know all essential properties (dhatiyyat) of a thing. This is an epistemic limitation. He says 'many attributes are hidden' and human capacity is 'very slight'.
This premise challenges the very possibility of exhaustive knowledge of essences.
3
Analyze the definer's dilemma
If a definer lists only the essential properties he knows, he cannot be certain that there is not another essential property he has overlooked. An opponent can always demand proof of completeness, and the definer cannot simply say 'I would have known if there were more' because many attributes are hidden.
The burden of proof is on the definer to show completeness, which is impossible to satisfy.
4
Examine the refutation of the counterargument
Suhrawardi considers the potential counterargument: if an additional essential property existed, we could not grasp the essence without it, so we are safe. He refutes this by stating that true knowledge of a reality requires knowledge of *all* its essential properties. If a possible essential property is omitted, the knowledge is uncertain, not certain.
Thus, the counterargument fails; uncertainty remains even if we think we have the essence.
5
State the conclusion
Suhrawardi concludes that producing a hadd in the Peripatetic sense is not possible. He notes that even Aristotle acknowledged the difficulty. The commentator al-Shirazi adds that the impossibility is either because of the risk of omission or the difficulty of distinguishing essential from non-essential properties.
The practical consequence is that logicians turn to rasm (description) using properties (khawass).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يهاجم السهروردي فكرة الذاتيات وقيام التعريف عليها . فيقول « من ذكر ما عرف من الذاتيات ـ لم يأمن وجود ذاتي آخر غفل عنه . وللمستشرح أو المنازع أن يطالبه بذلك . وليس للمعرف حينئذ أن يقول لو كانت صفة أخرى لاطلعت عليها إذ كثيرة الصفات غير ظاهرة » يقصد السهروردي بذلك أن الطاقة الإنسانية لا تستطيع الإحاطة بجميع ذاتيات الحقيقة التي يطلب حدها . فمعارف الإنسان ضئيلة تماماً مع عدم تمكنه الوصول إلى كنه كثير من الأشياء . أما التعلل بأنه لو كان للحقيقة ذاتي آخر ، ما استطعنا الوصول إلى الماهية بدونه ، وعلى هذا الأساس ينكرون أي ذاتي آخر للماهية ، فتعليل خاطىء « لأن الحقيقة إنما تكون عرفت ، إذا عرف جميع ذاتياتها . فإذا انقدح جواز ذاتي ، لم تكن معرفة الحقيقة متيقنة ، بل تكون مشكوكة . ويستخلص السهروردي من هذا أن الإتيان بالحد كما إلتزم المشاؤون ـ أي تركيبيه من الجنس والفصل ـ غير ممكن . ويعلل الشارح الشيرازي هذا إما بجواز الاخلال بذاتي لم يعرف ، وإما لصعوبة تمييز الأجناس والفصول من اللوازم العامة والخاصة . ولهذا عدل المشاؤون إلى الرسوم المؤلفة من الخواص . ويقرر السهروردي آخر الأمر أن « صاحب المشائين » أي أرسطوطاليس ـ اعترف بصعوبة الإتيان بالحد .
Then Suhrawardi attacks the idea of essential properties (dhatiyyat) and the foundation of definition upon them. He says: 'Whoever mentions what he knows of the essential properties is not safe from the existence of another essential property he neglected. The seeker of explanation or the disputant may demand that from him. And the definer cannot then say: if there were another attribute I would have been aware of it, for many attributes are hidden.' By this, Suhrawardi means that human capacity cannot encompass all essential properties of the reality whose limit is sought. So human knowledge is very slight, with their inability to reach the core of many things. As for the excuse that if the reality had another essential property, we could not reach the essence without it, and on this basis they deny any additional essential property of the essence, it is a false reasoning 'because the reality is only known when all its essential properties are known. So if the possibility of an essential property is posited, the knowledge of the reality is not certain, but is doubtful.' Suhrawardi concludes from this that producing the definition (hadd) as the Peripatetics committed to — i.e., composite of genus and differential — is not possible. The commentator al-Shirazi explains this either due to the possibility of omitting an unknown essential property, or due to the difficulty of distinguishing genera and differentiae from common and particular necessary concomitants. For this reason, the Peripatetics turned to descriptions (rusum) composed of properties. Suhrawardi lastly affirms that 'the master of the Peripatetics,' i.e., Aristotle, acknowledged the difficulty of producing the definition (hadd).
Source pages p. 303

Commentary
This passage presents Suhrawardi’s critical argument against the Aristotelian notion of definition (hadd) based on genus and differentia. Suhrawardi begins by positing a fundamental epistemological challenge: any definer who claims to list all essential properties (dhatiyyat) of a thing cannot be certain that no other essential property has been overlooked. An opponent could always demand proof of completeness, and the definer cannot simply appeal to his own awareness, because many attributes remain hidden. The core of Suhrawardi’s critique is that human cognitive capacity is limited and cannot fully grasp the inner reality of things. He then refutes a common counterargument: the claim that if an additional essential property existed, we would have been unable to grasp the essence without it, and therefore such an additional property can be denied. This, Suhrawardi explains, is false because true knowledge of a reality requires knowledge of all its essential properties; if there is a possibility of an unknown essential property, the knowledge becomes uncertain rather than certain. Consequently, the Peripatetic ideal of producing a definition composed of genus and differentia is impossible in practice. Al-Shirazi, the commentator, attributes this impossibility either to the risk of omitting an unknown essential property or to the difficulty of correctly distinguishing genera and differentiae from common and particular necessary concomitants (lawazim). For this reason, the Peripatetics themselves resorted to descriptive definitions (rusum) using properties (khawass). Suhrawardi finally notes that even Aristotle admitted the difficulty of producing a perfect definition. The passage does not contain a logical argument in syllogistic form; rather, it is a dialectical critique that questions the epistemological foundations of the Aristotelian theory of definition.

Key Terms
الذاتيات (al-dhatiyyat): Essential properties that constitute the quiddity (mahiyya) of a thing; the attributes without which the thing would not be what it is. [Note: In earlier Peripatetic logic, dhatiyyat were considered fully knowable; Suhrawardi here challenges that certainty.]
الحد (al-hadd): A definition composed of genus (jins) and differentia (fasl) that exhaustively states the essence. [Note: In the standard Aristotelian tradition, hadd is the ideal definition; Suhrawardi argues it is practically unattainable.]
الرسم (al-rasm): A description that uses non-essential properties (khawass) to identify a thing, serving as a fallback when hadd is impossible. [Note: No significant shift; rasm is consistently a lesser form of definition.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Suhrawardi, why is a Peripatetic definition (hadd) by genus and differentia impossible?
A) Because genus and differentia cannot be known at all.
B) Because human knowledge cannot be certain that all essential properties are known, and any missing property would make the definition uncertain.
C) Because the Peripatetics did not believe in essential properties.
D) Because Aristotle himself rejected the use of definition.

Key Takeaway
Suhrawardi's critique shows that perfect definition (hadd) is epistemologically unattainable, leading to the acceptance of descriptive definitions (rusum).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In a debate between a Peripatetic logician and Suhrawardi, the Peripatetic claims to have defined 'time' as 'the measure of motion.' Using Suhrawardi's argument, construct an objection: ask the Peripatetic to prove that no other essential property of time exists. What is the Peripatetic's likely response, and how would Suhrawardi refute it?
?
Identify a key term in your own field of study (e.g., 'jihad' in fiqh, 'sunna' in hadith). Can you be certain that your definition captures all its essential properties? Explain using Suhrawardi's reasoning.

Hadd and Rasm
beginner

Takhsis al-Ahad: Individual Specificity
Suhrawardi presents the first type of definition: takhsis al-ahad.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the takhsis al-ahad type of definition and explain why it results in an incomplete description (rasm naqis) because it lacks a genus.

Core Idea
When every attribute in a definition is individually specific, the definition still fails as a complete definition because it lacks a genus.

Suhrawardi's first type of definition, takhsis al-ahad, occurs when every part of the definiens is exclusive to the definiendum. For example, defining a human as 'rational, laughing, writing, thinking' uses attributes that are each unique to humans. However, as the commentator al-Shirazi points out, this type of definition contains no genus, making it an incomplete description (rasm naqis) in the standard logical framework. Understanding why this is insufficient is key to appreciating the role of genus in definition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum and the definiens
Locate the thing being defined (human) and the attributes used (rational, laughing, writing, thinking).
2
Check each attribute for specificity
Verify that each attribute is specific to humans, i.e., not shared with other species.
3
Determine the presence or absence of a genus
Note that the list contains no broader category (e.g., animal).
4
Classify the definition type
Recognize it as takhsis al-ahad, and then evaluate its logical status: the commentator calls it rasm naqis because it lacks a genus.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يرى السهروردي أن الشيء إذا عرف لمن يعرف فينبغي أن يكون التعريف بأمور تخصه إما لتخصيص الآحاد أو لتخصيص البعض أو للاجتماع . أما عبارة « لتخصيص الآحاد » فيشرحها الشارح « بأن يكون كل واحد من تلك الأمور التي هي أجزاء المعرف مختصاً بالشيء كقولنا في تعريف الإنسان إنه ناطق ضاحك كاتب متفكر » ويعتبر الشارح هذا التعريف إذا ما حاولنا أن نرده إلى صورة منطقية يونانية ، رسماً ناقصاً لخلوه عن الجنس . أما عبارة « لتخصيص البعض » فيشرحها الشارح « بأن يكون بعض أجزاء المعرف مختصاً بالمعرف دون البعض » ويعتبر الشارح هذا التعريف حداً تاماً أو رسماً تاماً إذا كان جزء المعرف غير المختص جنساً قريباً وجزء المعرف المختص إما فصل وإما خاصة . فإذا أردنا أن نعرف الإنسان قلنا إنه حيوان ناطق أو ضاحك . والتعريف الأول حد تام والثاني رسم تام . ويعتبره حداً ناقصاً ، أو رسماً ناقصاً إن كان الجزء المعرف غير المختص جنساً بعيداً ، أو ان كان الجزء المعرف المختص إما فصلاً وإما خاصة . فإذا أردنا أن نعرف الإنسان قلنا إنه جوهر ناطق أو ضاحك » ويرى الشارح هذا أيضاً إذا ما أبدلنا الجنس القريب بالعرض فنقول إنه ماش ناطق أو ضاحك . أما عبارة « أو للاجتماع » فيشرحها الشارح « بأن يكون التعريف بأمور لا تختص آحادها شيء ولا بعضها بل تخصه للاجتماع ـ هو أن يختص مجموعها بالشيء ـ دون شيء من أجزائه ويعتبر الشارح هذا التعريف رسماً ناقصاً ، لأنه بالخاصة المركبة أي ان اختصاصه انما يحصل بالتركيب . فنقول في تعريف الخفاش إنه طائر ولود وكل واحد من هذين الأمرين ـ أي أنه طائر وأنه ولود ـ أعم من الخفاش ومجموعهما يختص به » فالتعريف إذن يحتوي على ثلاثة أنواع : الأول ـ هو أن يكون بأمور تختص الشيء اما لتخصيص الآحاد او لتخصيص البعض أو للاجتماع وهذا النوع الأخير من التعاريف يعتبره السهروردي أكمل أنواعها . يفسر الشارح هذا ـ عن كتاب السهروردي المطارحات ـ بأنه ليس هناك إلا تعريفات بأمور تخص الاجتماع ، كما نقول في تعريف الإنسان ، إنه
Suhrawardi holds that when a thing is defined for one who does not know it, the definition should be by matters that are specific to it, either by specification of individuals, or by specification of some, or by [specification through] conjunction. As for the phrase 'by specification of individuals,' the commentator explains it as 'each one of those matters which are parts of the definiens being specific to the thing, like our saying in defining man: he is rational, laughing, writing, thinking.' The commentator considers this definition, if we try to reduce it to Greek logical form, an incomplete description (rasm naqis) because it lacks a genus. As for the phrase 'by specification of some,' the commentator explains it as 'some parts of the definiens being specific to the definiendum to the exclusion of others.' The commentator considers this definition a complete definition (hadd tam) or a complete description (rasm tam) if the non-specified part of the definiens is a proximate genus and the specified part is either a differentia or a property. So if we want to define man, we say: he is a rational or laughing animal. The first is a complete definition (hadd tam), the second a complete description (rasm tam). He considers it an incomplete definition or incomplete description if the non-specified part is a remote genus, or if the specified part is a differentia or a property. For example, if we define man as a rational or laughing substance. The commentator also holds this if we replace the proximate genus with an accident and say: he is a walking, rational, or laughing [being]. As for the phrase 'or by conjunction,' the commentator explains it as 'the definition being by matters none of which individually or partially are specific, but rather are specific through conjunction — i.e., the sum of them is specific to the thing, not any of its parts.' The commentator considers this definition an incomplete description (rasm naqis), because it is by a composite property (khassa murakkaba), i.e., its specificity occurs only through composition. So we say in defining the bat: it is a flying, viviparous [creature]. Each of these two matters — i.e., that it is flying and that it is viviparous — is more general than the bat, but their combination is specific to it.' Therefore, the definition contains three types: first, it is by matters that specify the thing, either by specification of individuals, or by specification of some, or by conjunction. This last type of definition is considered by Suhrawardi the most complete of them. The commentator explains this, from Suhrawardi's book al-Mutarahat, that there are only definitions by matters that are specific through conjunction, as we say in defining man: he is...
Source pages p. 303

Commentary
This passage sets out Suhrawardi’s classification of definitions, as explained by the commentator al-Shirazi. Suhrawardi states that when defining an unknown thing, the definition must consist of attributes (umur) that are particular to that thing. These attributes can be specific in three ways: (1) takhsis al-ahad (specification of individuals) — each individual attribute in the definition is specific to the thing, e.g., defining human as 'rational, laughing, writing, thinking.' The commentator notes that when reduced to standard logical form, this is an incomplete description (rasm naqis) because no genus is included. (2) takhsis al-ba'd (specification of some) — some of the attributes are specific and some are not. The commentator evaluates the logical status based on whether the non-specific part is a proximate genus (jins qarib) and the specific part is a differentia (fasl) or property (khassa). If so, it yields a complete definition (hadd tam) or complete description (rasm tam). Example: 'human is a rational animal' (hadd tam) or 'human is a laughing animal' (rasm tam). If the genus is remote (jins ba'id), the definition is incomplete. For instance, 'human is a rational substance' is an incomplete definition because 'substance' is a remote genus. Similarly, if one substitutes a proximate genus with an accident, e.g., 'walking rational being,' it is considered incomplete. (3) lil-ijtima' (by conjunction) — the attributes are not individually or partially specific, but their combination is unique to the definiendum. The commentator labels this as an incomplete description (rasm naqis) because it uses a composite property (khassa murakkaba). Example: defining 'bat' as 'flying viviparous' — each attribute alone is more general (many things fly or give birth), but together they are specific. According to Suhrawardi, this third type is the most complete form of definition, since it avoids the pretension of capturing essential properties (dhatiyyat) and relies on observable, composite properties. The commentator, citing Suhrawardi's al-Mutarahat, states that all definitions essentially reduce to this type, where specificity arises from conjunction. The passage is an enumeration of definition types with logical evaluation, thus it is a definitional passage. The commentary follows Suhrawardi’s order: first the three categories, then the analysis of each by the commentator, and concludes with the supremacy of the third type.

Key Terms
تخصيص الآحاد
رسم ناقص
جنس

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, why is the definition of human as 'rational, laughing, writing, thinking' considered an incomplete description (rasm naqis)?
A) Because it includes too many attributes.
B) Because it lacks a genus.
C) Because it uses Greek logical terms.
D) Because the attributes are not specific.

Key Takeaway
Takhsis al-ahad definitions, though using individually specific attributes, are incomplete because they omit the genus necessary for a complete definition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take a definition from fiqh, such as 'salah is the set of specific bowings and prostrations with takbir and taslim.' Identify whether this is takhsis al-ahad, takhsis al-ba'd, or takhsis al-ijtima. Then determine if it is a hadd or rasm and whether it is complete.

Takhsis al-Ba'd: Partial Specificity
Suhrawardi and his commentator discuss the second type: takhsis al-ba'd.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate a definition of the takhsis al-ba'd type and determine whether it yields a complete or incomplete definition based on the genus used.

Core Idea
The completeness of a mixed-specificity definition hinges on whether the non-specific part is a proximate or remote genus.

The second type of definition according to Suhrawardi, takhsis al-ba'd, involves a mix of specific and non-specific attributes. The commentator al-Shirazi explains that this type can yield a complete definition (hadd tam) or complete description (rasm tam) if the non-specific part is a proximate genus and the specific part is a differentia or property. However, if the genus is remote or replaced by an accident, the definition becomes incomplete. Understanding this gradation is crucial for constructing sound definitions in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parts of the definiens
Separate the attributes into specific and non-specific parts. For example, 'animal' is non-specific (shared), 'rational' is specific.
2
Identify the genus
Determine if the non-specific part is a genus and whether it is proximate (jins qarib) or remote (jins ba'id).
3
Identify the specific part
Check if it is a differentia (fasl) or a property (khassa).
4
Classify the definition
If proximate genus + differentia = hadd tam; proximate genus + property = rasm tam; remote genus = hadd naqis or rasm naqis.
5
Apply to the examples given
'Human is a rational animal' = hadd tam; 'Human is a laughing animal' = rasm tam; 'Human is a rational substance' = incomplete because substance is remote genus.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

According to Suhrawardi, this third type is the most complete form of definition, since it avoids the pretension of capturing essential properties (dhatiyyat) and relies on observable, composite properties. The commentator, citing Suhrawardi's al-Mutarahat, states that all definitions essentially reduce to this type, where specificity arises from conjunction. The passage is an enumeration of definition types with logical evaluation, thus it is a definitional passage. The commentary follows Suhrawardi’s order: first the three categories, then the analysis of each by the commentator, and concludes with the supremacy of the third type.

Takhsis al-Ijtima': Conjunctive Specificity
Suhrawardi's third type: takhsis al-ijtima' (conjunctive specificity).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to recognize definitions that rely on conjunctive specificity (takhsis al-ijtima') and explain why Suhrawardi considers them the most complete type.

Core Idea
When no single attribute is specific, but their combination is, the definition achieves accuracy through observable composite properties.

The third type of definition in Suhrawardi's classification, takhsis al-ijtima', involves attributes that are not individually or partially specific but become unique when combined. The commentator gives the example of defining a bat as a 'flying viviparous' creature. Neither 'flying' nor 'viviparous' alone is specific to bats, but together they are. Suhrawardi considers this the most complete type of definition because it avoids claims about essential natures and relies on empirically verifiable composite properties.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum and the composite property
For bat: 'flying viviparous' is the composite.
2
Check each single attribute
Determine if each attribute is more general than the definiendum (e.g., many things fly, many things give birth).
3
Check the combination
Confirm that the combination of the attributes is specific to the definiendum.
4
Classify the definition
Recognize as takhsis al-ijtima'. The commentator calls it rasm naqis because it uses a composite property, but Suhrawardi considers it the most complete.
5
Understand the rationale
Why Suhrawardi prefers this type: it avoids the need to know essences, relying on observable accidents.

يرى السهروردي أن الشيء إذا عرف لمن يعرف فينبغي أن يكون التعريف بأمور تخصه إما لتخصيص الآحاد أو لتخصيص البعض أو للاجتماع . أما عبارة « لتخصيص الآحاد » فيشرحها الشارح « بأن يكون كل واحد من تلك الأمور التي هي أجزاء المعرف مختصاً بالشيء كقولنا في تعريف الإنسان إنه ناطق ضاحك كاتب متفكر » ويعتبر الشارح هذا التعريف إذا ما حاولنا أن نرده إلى صورة منطقية يونانية ، رسماً ناقصاً لخلوه عن الجنس . أما عبارة « لتخصيص البعض » فيشرحها الشارح « بأن يكون بعض أجزاء المعرف مختصاً بالمعرف دون البعض » ويعتبر الشارح هذا التعريف حداً تاماً أو رسماً تاماً إذا كان جزء المعرف غير المختص جنساً قريباً وجزء المعرف المختص إما فصل وإما خاصة . فإذا أردنا أن نعرف الإنسان قلنا إنه حيوان ناطق أو ضاحك . والتعريف الأول حد تام والثاني رسم تام . ويعتبره حداً ناقصاً ، أو رسماً ناقصاً إن كان الجزء المعرف غير المختص جنساً بعيداً ، أو ان كان الجزء المعرف المختص إما فصلاً وإما خاصة . فإذا أردنا أن نعرف الإنسان قلنا إنه جوهر ناطق أو ضاحك » ويرى الشارح هذا أيضاً إذا ما أبدلنا الجنس القريب بالعرض فنقول إنه ماش ناطق أو ضاحك . أما عبارة « أو للاجتماع » فيشرحها الشارح « بأن يكون التعريف بأمور لا تختص آحادها شيء ولا بعضها بل تخصه للاجتماع ـ هو أن يختص مجموعها بالشيء ـ دون شيء من أجزائه ويعتبر الشارح هذا التعريف رسماً ناقصاً ، لأنه بالخاصة المركبة أي ان اختصاصه انما يحصل بالتركيب . فنقول في تعريف الخفاش إنه طائر ولود وكل واحد من هذين الأمرين ـ أي أنه طائر وأنه ولود ـ أعم من الخفاش ومجموعهما يختص به » فالتعريف إذن يحتوي على ثلاثة أنواع : الأول ـ هو أن يكون بأمور تختص الشيء اما لتخصيص الآحاد او لتخصيص البعض أو للاجتماع وهذا النوع الأخير من التعاريف يعتبره السهروردي أكمل أنواعها . يفسر الشارح هذا ـ عن كتاب السهروردي المطارحات ـ بأنه ليس هناك إلا تعريفات بأمور تخص الاجتماع ، كما نقول في تعريف الإنسان ، إنه
Suhrawardi holds that when a thing is defined for one who does not know it, the definition should be by matters that are specific to it, either by specification of individuals, or by specification of some, or by [specification through] conjunction. As for the phrase 'by specification of individuals,' the commentator explains it as 'each one of those matters which are parts of the definiens being specific to the thing, like our saying in defining man: he is rational, laughing, writing, thinking.' The commentator considers this definition, if we try to reduce it to Greek logical form, an incomplete description (rasm naqis) because it lacks a genus. As for the phrase 'by specification of some,' the commentator explains it as 'some parts of the definiens being specific to the definiendum to the exclusion of others.' The commentator considers this definition a complete definition (hadd tam) or a complete description (rasm tam) if the non-specified part of the definiens is a proximate genus and the specified part is either a differentia or a property. So if we want to define man, we say: he is a rational or laughing animal. The first is a complete definition (hadd tam), the second a complete description (rasm tam). He considers it an incomplete definition or incomplete description if the non-specified part is a remote genus, or if the specified part is a differentia or a property. For example, if we define man as a rational or laughing substance. The commentator also holds this if we replace the proximate genus with an accident and say: he is a walking, rational, or laughing [being]. As for the phrase 'or by conjunction,' the commentator explains it as 'the definition being by matters none of which individually or partially are specific, but rather are specific through conjunction — i.e., the sum of them is specific to the thing, not any of its parts.' The commentator considers this definition an incomplete description (rasm naqis), because it is by a composite property (khassa murakkaba), i.e., its specificity occurs only through composition. So we say in defining the bat: it is a flying, viviparous [creature]. Each of these two matters — i.e., that it is flying and that it is viviparous — is more general than the bat, but their combination is specific to it.' Therefore, the definition contains three types: first, it is by matters that specify the thing, either by specification of individuals, or by specification of some, or by conjunction. This last type of definition is considered by Suhrawardi the most complete of them. The commentator explains this, from Suhrawardi's book al-Mutarahat, that there are only definitions by matters that are specific through conjunction, as we say in defining man: he is...
Source pages p. 303

Commentary
This passage sets out Suhrawardi’s classification of definitions, as explained by the commentator al-Shirazi. Suhrawardi states that when defining an unknown thing, the definition must consist of attributes (umur) that are particular to that thing. These attributes can be specific in three ways: (1) takhsis al-ahad (specification of individuals) — each individual attribute in the definition is specific to the thing, e.g., defining human as 'rational, laughing, writing, thinking.' The commentator notes that when reduced to standard logical form, this is an incomplete description (rasm naqis) because no genus is included. (2) takhsis al-ba'd (specification of some) — some of the attributes are specific and some are not. The commentator evaluates the logical status based on whether the non-specific part is a proximate genus (jins qarib) and the specific part is a differentia (fasl) or property (khassa). If so, it yields a complete definition (hadd tam) or complete description (rasm tam). Example: 'human is a rational animal' (hadd tam) or 'human is a laughing animal' (rasm tam). If the genus is remote (jins ba'id), the definition is incomplete. For instance, 'human is a rational substance' is an incomplete definition because 'substance' is a remote genus. Similarly, if one substitutes a proximate genus with an accident, e.g., 'walking rational being,' it is considered incomplete. (3) lil-ijtima' (by conjunction) — the attributes are not individually or partially specific, but their combination is unique to the definiendum. The commentator labels this as an incomplete description (rasm naqis) because it uses a composite property (khassa murakkaba). Example: defining 'bat' as 'flying viviparous' — each attribute alone is more general (many things fly or give birth), but together they are specific. According to Suhrawardi, this third type is the most complete form of definition, since it avoids the pretension of capturing essential properties (dhatiyyat) and relies on observable, composite properties. The commentator, citing Suhrawardi's al-Mutarahat, states that all definitions essentially reduce to this type, where specificity arises from conjunction. The passage is an enumeration of definition types with logical evaluation, thus it is a definitional passage. The commentary follows Suhrawardi’s order: first the three categories, then the analysis of each by the commentator, and concludes with the supremacy of the third type.

خاصة مركبة
رسم ناقص

Key Takeaway

Three Types of Definition by Specification
Commentator classifies definitions by three modes of specification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any definition into one of the three types (takhsis al-ahad, takhsis al-ba'd, al-ijtima') and determine whether it is a complete definition (hadd), complete description (rasm), or incomplete.

Core Idea
Definitions are classified by how their parts specify the thing: individually, partially, or through aggregation.

The commentator lays out a classification of definitions based on how the constituent matters (umur) relate to the definiendum. Understanding this taxonomy is essential because it determines whether a definition is a complete logical definition (hadd), a complete description (rasm tamm), or an incomplete one. This classification underpins rigorous definition in all Islamic sciences, from fiqh to kalam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of specification
The commentator introduces three ways a definition's parts can be specific: (1) each part individually specific (takhsis al-ahad), (2) only some parts specific (takhsis al-ba'd), (3) only the combination of all parts is specific (al-ijtima').
The examples given are: for type 1, 'man is rational, laughing, writing, thinking'; for type 2, 'man is animal rational'; for type 3, 'bat is flying, viviparous'.
2
Extract the genus and differentia for type 2 definitions
In takhsis al-ba'd, the non-specific part is a genus (jins). If it is a proximate genus and the specific part is a differentia (fasl), the definition is a complete hadd. If the specific part is a property (khassa), it is a complete rasm.
For example: 'animal rational' is a complete hadd; 'animal laughing' is a complete rasm.
3
Assess completeness based on genus proximity
If the non-specific part is a remote genus (e.g., 'substance' instead of 'animal'), the definition becomes incomplete (hadd naqis or rasm naqis). Similarly, replacing the genus with an accident yields incompleteness.
The commentator gives: 'substance rational' as an incomplete hadd.
4
Classify aggregate definitions (type 3)
For definitions by aggregation, none of the parts individually are specific, but their sum is. The commentator calls this an incomplete rasm because it relies on a composite property. However, Suhrawardi later argues it is the most complete type.
Example: 'bat is flying, viviparous' – each alone is more general, but together they uniquely specify the bat.
