(۲) شرح .... ص ٥٢ . (۳) شرح .... ص ۷۲ . ۳۰۷
Definition is by matters that are specific to it, either by specifying individuals, or by specifying some, or by aggregation. As for the phrase 'by specifying individuals', the commentator explains it: 'that each one of those matters which are parts of the definiendum is specific to the thing, as when we say in the definition of man: he is rational, laughing, writing, thinking.' And the commentator considers this definition, if we try to reduce it to a Greek logical form, an incomplete description (rasm naqis) because it lacks the genus. As for the phrase 'by specifying some', the commentator explains it: 'that some parts of the definiendum are specific to the definiendum and not others.' And the commentator considers this definition a complete definition (hadd tam) or a complete description (rasm tam) if the non-specific part of the definiendum is a proximate genus and the specific part is either a differentia or a property. So if we want to define man, we say: he is a rational animal or a laughing animal. The first definition is a complete definition, the second a complete description. And he considers it an incomplete definition, or an incomplete description, if the non-specific part of the definiendum is a remote genus, or if the specific part is either a differentia or a property. So if we want to define man, we say: he is a rational substance or a laughing substance. And the commentator also holds this if we replace the proximate genus with an accident, saying: he is a walking, rational or laughing being. As for the phrase 'or by aggregation', the commentator explains it: 'that the definition is by matters whose individuals are not specific nor are some of them specific, but it is specific by aggregation — that is, their sum is specific to the thing — and not any of its parts.' And the commentator considers this definition an incomplete description, because it is by a composite property, i.e., its specificity is attained only by composition. So we say in the definition of bat: it is a flying, viviparous creature; each of these two matters — that it is flying and that it is viviparous — is more general than the bat, but their combination is specific to it. (1) So definition contains three types: the first is that it be by matters that are specific to the thing either by specifying individuals or by specifying some or by aggregation; and this last type of definitions Suhrawardi considers the most complete of them. The commentator explains this — from Suhrawardi's book al-Mutarahat — that there are only definitions by matters that are specific by aggregation, as we say in the definition of man: he is erect in stature, fair-skinned, broad-nailed. For each of these matters, although its existence in other than man is possible, yet it is specific to him to the exclusion of others, among what we know of essences; and through it his distinction is obtained, and the possibility that the combination exists in another essence that we do not know does not harm it. And he holds that this difficulty is only in the definition according to essence and reality, not according to concept and consideration. For if one expresses man as 'the laughing animal, erect in stature, fair-skinned', it is a complete definition that does not prevent agreement upon it, and it is not permissible to replace it by saying: he is a rational animal, broad-nailed. For each one of what is mentioned in the first is essential (dhati) according to concept and consideration, and it is not permissible to replace the essentials of the definition nor to increase or decrease them. And this, according to Suhrawardi, is not a description (rasm), because it is by concomitants (lawazim), and the one who defines by description knows that this name is not for these predicates, but for something to which the mind transfers from them, unlike the definer according to conceptual consideration, for the name in his view is for the sum of these predicates, all of which are essential according to the concept. (1) And the commentator reports from Suhrawardi that the conceptual definition benefits in the sciences a benefit not less than that according to essence and reality. (2) And he repeats this in another paragraph as well. Indeed, the conceptual definition - in his view - is more correct than the definition according to reality and has no difficulty, because the definition according to reality, as he mentions, may have its existence occur in another essence unknown to the definer, just as it is possible to omit an essential of the thing that he has not discovered. In general, fallacies of definition are frequent in this type. And Suhrawardi distinguishes between the definition according to concept and consideration and the description, because the description is obtained by concomitants, whereas the conceptual definition is the set of essential predicates that are applied to the thing according to the concept. (3) Is there a relation between the definition according to concept and consideration and the definition by name? In reality, there is no relation at all. Commentary
This passage is a detailed classification of definitions in logic, presented by a commentator and then refined with Suhrawardi's perspective. The commentator begins by stating that a definition can be composed of matters that are specific to the definiendum in one of three ways: (1) each matter individually is specific to the definiendum, e.g., defining man as 'rational, laughing, writing, thinking'—but this is considered an incomplete description (rasm naqis) because it lacks a genus (jins). (2) Some matters are specific while others are not; if the non-specific part is a proximate genus and the specific is a differentia or property, it yields a complete definition (hadd tam) or complete description (rasm tam). For example, 'animal rational' is a complete definition, 'animal laughing' is a complete description. If the genus is remote or the accidental part is misplaced, it becomes incomplete. (3) Aggregation of non-specific matters: e.g., defining bat as 'flying, viviparous'—each part alone is more general, but their combination is specific. The commentator calls this an incomplete description because it relies on a composite property. Suhrawardi then argues that this third type is the most complete. He introduces a crucial distinction between definition according to essence (hadd bi-hasab al-haqiqa wal-mahiyya) and definition according to concept (hadd bi-hasab al-mafhum wal-'inaya). The latter, he says, avoids the difficulty of unknown essences and is more useful in sciences. For example, defining man as 'laughing animal, erect, fair-skinned' is a complete definition according to concept because each attribute is essential (dhati) in that context. This is not a mere description (rasm) because description relies on concomitants (lawazim), whereas conceptual definition uses essential predicates. Suhrawardi further states that conceptual definition is more correct than essential definition, which may accidentally apply to an unknown essence or miss an essential attribute. Finally, the passage notes that conceptual definition has no relation to definition by name (bi-hasab al-ism), which is a purely nominal definition. The commentator's exposition clarifies the logical taxonomy of definitions and Suhrawardi's innovative emphasis on the conceptual approach.

B) takhsis al-ba'd

Key Takeaway
The three types of definition—by individual specification, partial specification, and aggregate specification—determine whether a definition is a complete logical definition or a mere description.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Classify the following definition of 'salat' (prayer): 'Salat is the act of bowing, prostrating, reciting Quranic verses, and performing tashahhud.' Determine which type of specification it belongs to and whether it is a complete definition, complete description, or incomplete.

Construct a complete definition (hadd tam) of 'zakat' using a proximate genus and an essential differentia from Islamic law.

Hadd and Rasm
intermediate
10 min
Suhrawardi's Conceptual Definition and Its Superiority
Suhrawardi refines definition with conceptual definition, arguing for its superiority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between definition according to essence (hadd bi-hasab al-haqiqa) and definition according to concept (hadd bi-hasab al-mafhum), and explain why Suhrawardi considers the latter more useful and reliable in the sciences.

Core Idea
Suhrawardi's conceptual definition avoids the pitfalls of essential definition by using known essential predicates.

Suhrawardi introduces a novel approach to definition: rather than trying to capture the metaphysical essence (haqiqa) of a thing—which may be unknown or misidentified—he defines by the set of essential predicates that are clear in our mental concept (mafhum). This shift has profound implications for how definitions are constructed and evaluated in Islamic sciences, especially where essences are hidden or debated.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the problem with essential definitions
Suhrawardi points out that a definition according to essence (hadd bi-hasab al-haqiqa) may accidentally apply to an unknown essence (the definiens might fit something else we don't know) or may miss an essential attribute that we haven't discovered.
He says: 'the definition according to reality may have its existence occur in another essence unknown to the definer, just as it is possible to omit an essential of the thing that he has not discovered.'
2
Define the conceptual definition (hadd mafhumi)
The conceptual definition is the set of essential predicates (dhatiyat) that are applied to the thing according to the mental concept (bi-hasab al-mafhum wal-'inaya). It does not claim to capture the ontological essence, only the meaning of the name.
Example: defining man as 'laughing animal, erect in stature, fair-skinned' – each attribute is considered essential for the concept even if not metaphysically essential.
3
Distinguish conceptual definition from description (rasm)
Suhrawardi insists that conceptual definition is not a description, because descriptions use concomitants (lawazim) that lead the mind to the thing, while conceptual definition treats the predicates as directly naming the concept.
In a description, the name refers to something beyond the predicates; in conceptual definition, the name refers to the combination of predicates themselves.
4
Assess the advantages of conceptual definition
Suhrawardi claims that conceptual definition is more correct and useful in sciences because it avoids the difficulties of essential definition: no risk of misidentifying essences, and it relies on known attributes that can be agreed upon.
He says: 'the conceptual definition benefits in the sciences a benefit not less than that according to essence and reality' and is 'more correct than the definition according to reality and has no difficulty.'

Definition is by matters that are specific to it, either by specifying individuals, or by specifying some, or by aggregation. As for the phrase 'by specifying individuals', the commentator explains it: 'that each one of those matters which are parts of the definiendum is specific to the thing, as when we say in the definition of man: he is rational, laughing, writing, thinking.' And the commentator considers this definition, if we try to reduce it to a Greek logical form, an incomplete description (rasm naqis) because it lacks the genus. As for the phrase 'by specifying some', the commentator explains it: 'that some parts of the definiendum are specific to the definiendum and not others.' And the commentator considers this definition a complete definition (hadd tam) or a complete description (rasm tam) if the non-specific part of the definiendum is a proximate genus and the specific part is either a differentia or a property. So if we want to define man, we say: he is a rational animal or a laughing animal. The first definition is a complete definition, the second a complete description. And he considers it an incomplete definition, or an incomplete description, if the non-specific part of the definiendum is a remote genus, or if the specific part is either a differentia or a property. So if we want to define man, we say: he is a rational substance or a laughing substance. And the commentator also holds this if we replace the proximate genus with an accident, saying: he is a walking, rational or laughing being. As for the phrase 'or by aggregation', the commentator explains it: 'that the definition is by matters whose individuals are not specific nor are some of them specific, but it is specific by aggregation — that is, their sum is specific to the thing — and not any of its parts.' And the commentator considers this definition an incomplete description, because it is by a composite property, i.e., its specificity is attained only by composition. So we say in the definition of bat: it is a flying, viviparous creature; each of these two matters — that it is flying and that it is viviparous — is more general than the bat, but their combination is specific to it. (1) So definition contains three types: the first is that it be by matters that are specific to the thing either by specifying individuals or by specifying some or by aggregation; and this last type of definitions Suhrawardi considers the most complete of them. The commentator explains this — from Suhrawardi's book al-Mutarahat — that there are only definitions by matters that are specific by aggregation, as we say in the definition of man: he is erect in stature, fair-skinned, broad-nailed. And he holds that this difficulty is only in the definition according to essence and reality, not according to concept and consideration. For if one expresses man as 'the laughing animal, erect in stature, fair-skinned', it is a complete definition that does not prevent agreement upon it, and it is not permissible to replace it by saying: he is a rational animal, broad-nailed. For each one of what is mentioned in the first is essential (dhati) according to concept and consideration, and it is not permissible to replace the essentials of the definition nor to increase or decrease them. And this, according to Suhrawardi, is not a description (rasm), because it is by concomitants (lawazim), and the one who defines by description knows that this name is not for these predicates, but for something to which the mind transfers from them, unlike the definer according to conceptual consideration, for the name in his view is for the sum of these predicates, all of which are essential according to the concept. Commentary
This passage is a detailed classification of definitions in logic, presented by a commentator and then refined with Suhrawardi's perspective. The commentator begins by stating that a definition can be composed of matters that are specific to the definiendum in one of three ways: (1) each matter individually is specific to the definiendum, e.g., defining man as 'rational, laughing, writing, thinking'—but this is considered an incomplete description (rasm naqis) because it lacks a genus (jins). (2) Some matters are specific while others are not; if the non-specific part is a proximate genus and the specific is a differentia or property, it yields a complete definition (hadd tam) or complete description (rasm tam). For example, 'animal rational' is a complete definition, 'animal laughing' is a complete description. If the genus is remote or the accidental part is misplaced, it becomes incomplete. (3) Aggregation of non-specific matters: e.g., defining bat as 'flying, viviparous'—each part alone is more general, but their combination is specific. The commentator calls this an incomplete description because it relies on a composite property. Suhrawardi then argues that this third type is the most complete. He introduces a crucial distinction between definition according to essence (hadd bi-hasab al-haqiqa wal-mahiyya) and definition according to concept (hadd bi-hasab al-mafhum wal-'inaya). The latter, he says, avoids the difficulty of unknown essences and is more useful in sciences. For example, defining man as 'laughing animal, erect, fair-skinned' is a complete definition according to concept because each attribute is essential (dhati) in that context. This is not a mere description (rasm) because description relies on concomitants (lawazim), whereas conceptual definition uses essential predicates. Suhrawardi further states that conceptual definition is more correct than essential definition, which may accidentally apply to an unknown essence or miss an essential attribute. Finally, the passage notes that conceptual definition has no relation to definition by name (bi-hasab al-ism), which is a purely nominal definition. The commentator's exposition clarifies the logical taxonomy of definitions and Suhrawardi's innovative emphasis on the conceptual approach.

Hadd and Rasm
intermediate
7 min
Suhrawardi's Definition of Man by Conceptual Essential Attributes
Suhrawardi defines human by essential attributes and distinguishes his hadd from rasm.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish Suhrawardi's conceptual essential definition (al-hadd bi-ḥasab al-mafhūm wa al-ʿināya) from the traditional real definition (al-hadd al-ḥaqīqī) and the description (al-rasm).

Core Idea
Suhrawardi redefines definition: essential is about concept, not metaphysical quiddity.

In this passage, Suhrawardi proposes a novel approach to definition, using human as an example. He argues that a definition can be composed of multiple conceptually essential attributes, not just genus and differentia. This challenges the Aristotelian tradition and raises a distinction between hadd and rasm based on the nature of the attributes used.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
The term being defined is 'human' (al-insān). Find the Arabic word in the passage.
2
Extract the attributes listed
The attributes are: erect of stature, bare of skin, broad of nails, and laughing (implied in 'animal laughing'). List them all.
Suhrawardi uses four attributes: laughing, erect stature, bare skin, broad nails.
3
Understand Suhrawardi's criterion for essential
Each attribute is 'essential according to concept and attention' (dhātī bi-ḥasab al-mafhūm wa al-ʿināya). This means they are conceptually inseparable from the idea of human, even if not metaphysically constitutive.
This differs from Aristotelian essentialism where only genus and differentia are essential.
4
Compare with traditional hadd
Traditional hadd uses one genus and one differentia (e.g., rational animal). Suhrawardi's definition uses multiple attributes without a single differentia.
Suhrawardi's definition is a hadd according to concept, not according to reality (ḥaqīqa).
5
Distinguish from rasm
Rasm uses accidental properties (lāzim) that are not essential. Suhrawardi insists his attributes are essential conceptually, so his definition is a hadd, not a rasm.
The person giving a rasm knows the name refers to something beyond the predicates; Suhrawardi's name refers directly to the sum of predicates.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المنتصب القامة البادي البشرة العريض الأظفار ، لأن كلاً من هذه الأمور ، وإن جاز وجودها في غير الإنسان ، لكن تختص به دون غيره، مما نعرفه من الماهيات : وما به يتحصل تميزه ، ولا يقدح فيه جواز كون المجموع في ماهية أخرى لا نعرفها . ويرى أن هذه الصعوبة إنما هي في الحد بحسب الحقيقة والماهية لا بحسب المفهوم والعناية ، فإنه إذا عبر عن الإنسان بالحيوان الضاحك المنتصب القامة البادي البشرة ، كان حداً تاماً لا يمنع عن الاصطلاح عليه ، ولا يجوز تبديله بأن يقال : هو حيوان ناطق عريض الأظفار . فان كل واحد مما ذكر في الأول ذاتي بحسب المفهوم والعناية ، ولا يجوز تبديل ذاتيات الحد ولا الزيادة ولا النقصان فيها : وهذا عند السهروردي ليس برسم ، لأنه باللوازم ، والراسم يعرف أن هذا الاسم ليس لهذه المحمولات ، بل لأمر ينتقل الذهن منها إليه ، بخلاف الحاد بحسب العناية ، فإن الاسم عنده لمجموع هذه المحمولات التي كلها ذاتي بحسب المفهوم(۱)
The erect of stature, the bare of skin, the broad of nails, because each of these matters, even if it is possible for it to exist in other than human, nevertheless is specific to him to the exclusion of others, from what we know of the essences: and that by which his distinction is achieved, and the possibility of the sum existing in another essence we do not know does not invalidate it. And he sees that this difficulty is only in the definition according to reality and essence, not according to concept and attention, for if one describes human as the laughing animal, erect of stature, bare of skin, it would be a complete definition that does not prevent being agreed upon, and it is not permissible to replace it by saying: he is a rational animal broad of nails. For each one mentioned in the first is essential according to concept and attention, and it is not permissible to replace the essential parts of the definition nor to increase or decrease them: and this according to Suhrawardi is not a descriptive definition (rasm), because it uses necessary accidents, and the one giving the descriptive definition knows that this name is not for these predicates, but for an affair that the mind transfers from them to it, contrary to the definition according to attention, for the name according to him is for the sum of these predicates, all of which are essential according to concept.
Source pages p. 305

Commentary
This passage presents Suhrawardi's theory of definition. He proposes that a thing can be defined by a set of essential attributes that are conceptually inseparable from it, using the example of the human definition. He distinguishes this from the Aristotelian definition based on genus and differentia (hadd haqiqi). He also stresses that his definition is not a rasm, because rasm uses accidental properties, while his uses essential ones. The commentator notes Suhrawardi's own criteria: the predicates are essential in terms of concept and attention, not necessarily in terms of metaphysical essence.

Key Takeaway
